    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Everyone feels anxious sometimes, but generalized anxiety disorder is a condition in which a person has nearly constant anxiety. People with generalized anxiety disorder often feel anxious for no apparent reason. They also may feel tired, restless, irritable, short of breath, have difficulty concentrating or sleeping, or have a racing heartbeat. Doctors aren't sure what causes generalized anxiety disorder, but it can run in families. Stressful life situations also may cause the disorder. It can start anytime in life, even in childhood. Medications and psychotherapy, along with lifestyle changes, can help."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "In many cases, generalized anxiety disorder begins early in life, though it can start at any age. It usually develops more slowly than other types of anxiety disorders. It can make you feel completely consumed by worry or anxious for no reason. It often can occur with substance abuse and other types of mental illness such as depression. Generalized anxiety disorder usually is treated with a combination of medications and cognitive-behavioral therapy."

      "content": "About 6 million adults in the U.S. have generalized anxiety disorder."

      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Alcohol withdrawal happens when someone who drinks a lot of alcohol every day suddenly stops drinking. Symptoms can include anxiety, depression, headaches, nightmares, fatigue, sweating, and shakiness. Severe symptoms can include confusion and hallucinations (delirium tremens) and seizures. The symptoms of alcohol withdrawal can range from mild to severe depending on how heavily you drink. In severe cases, the symptoms can quickly become life threatening."

The symptoms can last for several weeks but are usually at their worst 48-72 hours after the last drink. You may have more serious symptoms if you also have another medical condition and should see your doctor. In some cases, you may need treatment in a hospital."

  "Alcohol_intoxication": [
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "Agitation\n\t\t•\tDouble vision (without one eye covered) \n\t\t•\tStool leaking (incontinence) \n\t\t•\tUrine leaking (incontinence) \n\t\t•\tSnoring\n\t\t•\tAbnormal gait (walking) \n\t\t•\tImpaired judgement\n\t\t•\tDifficulty walking\n\t\t•\tLoss of balance\n\t\t•\tMemory problems\n\t\t•\tSlurred speech\n\t\t•\tLoss of coordination\n\t\t•\tDrowsiness\n\t\t•\tJerking eye movements"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Alcohol Intoxication Overview",
      "content": "Alcohol intoxication occurs after you drink enough alcohol to change your behavior and impair your physical and mental abilities. If you have a blood alcohol concentration (BAC) of .08, you are legally intoxicated. Depending on their weight, most people hit .08 BAC after 2 to 3 drinks in 1 hour. But responses to alcohol vary from person to person. Some may become intoxicated on less alcohol. It depends upon how your body absorbs and gets rid of alcohol. Alcohol poisoning happens when you drink a lot of alcohol in a short amount of time. It is life-threatening and needs emergency medical care. Binge drinking is often the cause of alcohol poisoning."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Immediately after you drink, your BAC rises sharply and may lead to feelings of pleasure, relaxation, and greater sociability. But with more drinking, behavior varies. Some people become withdrawn. Others are more lively. Still others become angry or aggressive. Generally, the physical and mental effects peak about 45 to 90 minutes after you stop drinking. BAC then quickly declines, falling more slowly until alcohol completely leaves the body several hours later. Exactly when this happens depends in part upon how much you drink."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "About two out of three American adults drink alcoholic beverages. Nearly 17.6 million of them have problems with alcohol or are alcoholics."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "There is no treatment to reverse alcohol intoxication. However, for severe alcohol intoxication, you may receive: \n\t\t•\tIntravenous (IV) fluids with B complex vitamins for dehydration \n\t\t•\tA breathing tube \n\t\t•\tFollow-up to monitor recovery until BAC falls."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "Seek emergency medical help if you suspect that someone may have alcohol poisoning. \n\t\t•\tRemove the person from the alcohol. \n\t\t•\tDo not let the person drive. \n\t\t•\tKeep the person in a safe place to help prevent falls or dangerous objects. \n\t\t•\tPosition the person on his or her side, in case of vomiting. \n\t\t•\tMake sure you can easily arouse the person by talking or with a slight shake. \n\t\t•\tHave someone stay with the person to make sure he or she gets better. \n\t\t•\tAvoid coffee or cold showers, which have little effect."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Eating little food beforehand, taking other drugs, drinking faster, stronger drinks"
    },
    {
      "heading": "When to See Your Doctor",
      "content": "It's common to vomit when intoxicated. But if vomiting occurs more than once or if the person is unable to express thoughts clearly, take him or her to the emergency room. Call 911 if you can't wake the person up, breathing is very slow or stops and starts, the person has a seizure, the skin is very pale or blue, or the skin feels cold. Do the same if there is any doubt about the person's condition."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Questions to Ask Your Doctor",
      "content": "What are the risks of alcohol? \n\t\t•\tWhat should I do if someone close to me becomes intoxicated? \n\t\t•\tDo you think I have a drinking problem? \n\t\t•\tWhere can I go to get help? \n\t\t•\tIf I keep drinking, am I at risk for any complications?"

      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "People who have a serious head injury can develop dementia. They may have memory problems, difficulty speaking, and personality changes. Some people with dementia after a head injury get better over months and years. Others don't. Motor vehicle accidents are the most common cause of a single head injury that leads to dementia. Repeated blows to the head as from boxing or playing football can cause dementia symptoms that will not emerge until years later. This is called chronic traumatic encephalopathy. Treatment focuses on helping the person adjust and relieving symptoms. Anyone who has just had a potentially serious head injury should go to the emergency room."

  "Drug_withdrawal": [
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "Agitation\n\t\t•\tFever"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Drug withdrawal occurs when you suddenly stop taking a drug you've become physically or psychologically addicted to. The drug can be an illicit street drug, a legal substance like alcohol, or a prescription medication you've become dependent on. Symptoms vary from drug to drug and from person to person. With certain drugs, such as alcohol or tranquilizers, complications of withdrawal can be life-threatening. They might include heart attack, seizure, or stroke. With other drugs such as heroin, withdrawal can be extremely uncomfortable, but not life-threatening. Acute withdrawal occurs shortly after you stop using a drug. Physical symptoms are often very intense and can last from a few days to a few weeks. That's followed by a stage called post-acute withdrawal. At this stage, symptoms occur episodically and are primarily psychological. They can include symptoms such as trouble sleeping, headaches, confusion, and depression. Post-acute withdrawal can last up to a couple of years. Relapse can be a major complication during this phase."
    },
    {
      "heading": "EMERGENCY",
      "content": "Go to the emergency room or call 911 if you are having drug withdrawal and experience seizures, fever, severe confusion, hallucinations, or irregular heartbeat."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Withdrawal occurs in two stages: acute and post-acute. The first, or acute, stage can last up to a few weeks. Symptoms of acute withdrawal depend on what drug you have been taking and also can vary from person to person. They can include emotional symptoms such as anxiety, headaches, an inability to concentrate, and depression. They can also include physical symptoms such as sweating, difficulty breathing, abnormal heart rhythms, nausea, and other symptoms. Certain drugs, such as alcohol and tranquilizers, can cause more serious symptoms such as seizure, heart attack, and stroke. During the post-acute stage, the symptoms are less physical and more emotional. Post-acute symptoms typically occur in episodes that last a few days and then go away -- only to come back. This can go on for a couple of years while you recover."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "Treatment depends on the specific drug that's been used. Typically, medications are used to reduce craving and treat symptoms. Other treatments will address specific complications. After withdrawal, behavioral therapy, either on an inpatient or an outpatient basis, is needed to help prevent a return to drug use."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "Drug withdrawal should be treated by a medical professional. During post-acute withdrawal and treatment for addiction, you can improve your chances of success by: \n\t\t•\tAvoiding high-risk situations and associations \n\t\t•\tLearning to relax \n\t\t•\tBeing honest with yourself and your supporters about your problem with drugs"
    },
    {
      "heading": "When to See Your Doctor",
      "content": "Drug withdrawal can sometimes have life-threatening complications. Call your doctor or go to an emergency room if you think you may be experiencing drug withdrawal."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Questions to Ask Your Doctor",
      "content": "\t\t•\t Are my symptoms a result of drug withdrawal? \n\t\t•\tHow long can I expect the symptoms to last? \n\t\t•\tIs there anything I can do to get relief from the symptoms? \n\t\t•\tAfter withdrawal will I need any further treatment? If so, how can I get it?"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Diagnosed By",
      "content": "In most cases, a doctor can diagnose drug withdrawal with a physical exam and questions about your medical history and drug use. The doctor may also ask for blood and urine tests to confirm the diagnosis."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "Nearly 24.6 million Americans age 12 and over were using illicit drugs in 2013. Of that number, 4.8 million used drugs other than marijuana."
    },

      "heading": "Risk Factors",
      "content": "Suddenly stopping drugs or alcohol use, substance abuse, a physical dependence on drugs or alcohol"

  "Epilepsy_(temporal_lobe)": [
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "\t\t•\t Agitation\n\t\t•\tConfusion\n\t\t•\tMood swings\n\t\t•\tParanoid behavior\n\t\t•\tUnusual behavior\n\t\t•\tInappropriate behavior\n\t\t•\tInvoluntary movements (picking, lip smacking etc.) \n\t\t•\tAnxiety\n\t\t•\tPersonality changes\n\t\t•\tSense of impending doom\n\t\t•\tHallucinations\n\t\t•\tFrightening thoughts\n\t\t•\tHearing voices\n\t\t•\tStrange smell or taste"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Epilepsy is a condition marked by seizures. Temporal lobe seizures originate in the part of the brain that processes emotions and short-term memory, among other functions. Unlike other forms of seizures, temporal lobe seizures often don't respond to antiseizure medications. Surgery may be the only treatment option. For many people, surgery can be used to stop seizures."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "If your doctor identifies an underlying cause, such as an infection or a tumor, treating the condition may stop your seizures for good. For some forms of epilepsy, doctors prescribe an anticonvulsant drug to prevent seizures. Temporal lobe epilepsy doesn't always respond to these medications, however. Then the treatment of choice is surgery to remove the abnormal area of the brain. Surgery is successful in eliminating seizures in 70%-80% of people with temporal lobe epilepsy."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "One in 10 adults experiences a seizure sometime during his or her lifetime. Two million people in the U.S. have epilepsy. About half of patients with epilepsy have partial epilepsy, often originating in the temporal lobe."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Risk Factors",
      "content": "Some types of epilepsy can be inherited. Other types may result from a severe infection or head injury. Reducing your risk of a head injury by wearing a helmet when riding a bike or engaging in risky sports will lower your risk of injuries that can lead to seizures. Other risk factors include oxygen deprivation, brain infections, stroke, Alzheimer's and other neurologic disease, and brain tumors. Two out of three cases of epilepsy have no known cause."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "If an underlying cause is found, treatment will depend on the cause. If no cause is discovered, your doctor may prescribe an oral medication to help prevent seizures. \n\nThe type of drug and the dosage will depend on the kind of seizures you experience. Some people need to take medication for the rest of their lives. Others can reduce or completely stop taking medications if they go for several years without having another seizure. \n\nIn some cases, brain surgery can be performed to remove a small part of the brain that may be causing certain kinds of seizures. \n\nIf medications don't work and surgery isn't possible, your doctor may recommend vagus nerve stimulation. This treatment involves implanting an electrical device that stimulates a large nerve in the neck. \n\nCertain diets also may help reduce the chances of having seizures."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "Eating a healthy diet, getting plenty of sleep, and avoiding alcohol and illegal drugs may lower your risk of having a seizure."
    },
    {
      "heading": "When to See Your Doctor",
      "content": "Call your doctor if you have just experienced a seizure. If you already have been diagnosed with epilepsy, call your doctor if any new symptoms appear. \n\nCall 911 or your local emergency number if someone is having a seizure for the first time. If the victim is wearing a medical ID bracelet, follow the instructions on it. If someone with a history of epilepsy is having a seizure, call 911 if:\n\t\t•\tThe seizure lasts longer than five minutes without showing signs of slowing down \n\t\t•\tThe person has trouble breathing afterward \n\t\t•\tThe person is injured or in pain \n\t\t•\tRepeated seizures occur over a few minutes \n\t\t•\tThe victim does not regain consciousness or act normally between seizure"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Questions to Ask Your Doctor",
      "content": "\t\t•\t Was my seizure caused by a condition that can be corrected? \n\t\t•\tWhat kind of seizure did I experience? \n\t\t•\tWill there be lasting damage from the seizure? \n\t\t•\tIs there a problem with my brain's electrical system? \n\t\t•\tAm I likely to have more seizures? \n\t\t•\tWhat can I do to prevent seizures? \n\t\t•\tHow long will I need to take medication? \n\t\t•\tIs surgery an alternative for me?"
    },

  "Hypocalcemia": [
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "\t\t•\t Agitation\n\t\t•\tMuscle cramps or spasms (painful) \n\t\t•\tMuscle twitching (painless) \n\t\t•\tHeadache\n\t\t•\tMuscle weakness\n\t\t•\tDifficulty relaxing muscles after contracting them"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Hypocalcemia is a rare condition in which there is not enough calcium in the blood. Calcium is important for healthy bones and teeth, but also for blood clotting and nerve and muscle functions. Severe hypocalcemia can lead to cardiovascular collapse and low blood pressure that doesn't respond to treatment. Kidney failure, low levels of vitamin D or magnesium, some medication and blood disorders, chemotherapy, alcoholism, and underactive parathyroid glands can cause it. You may have symptoms such as tingling, muscle spasms and aches, and confusion. Raising your calcium levels, sometime intravenously, will clear it up. Death from hypocalcemia is very rare."
    },
    {
      "heading": "EMERGENCY",
      "content": "Hypocalcemia is a medical emergency. If you have severe muscle cramping, spasms, seizures, or any other symptom of hypocalcemia immediately see your doctor or go to a hospital emergency room."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "If you have long-standing hypocalcemia, you may not have any symptoms. Your doctor may discover it only during a routine blood test. If your hypocalcemia is acute and your blood calcium level drops quickly, your muscles or nerves may start to twitch. With mild hypocalcemia, you may have numbness and tingling around your mouth or in your fingers and toes and be depressed and irritable. With severe hypocalcemia, you may be confused and disoriented. Your heart muscles may contract irregularly because of electrolyte imbalance. For chronic hypocalcemia, calcium and vitamin D supplements probably will be enough to bring your calcium levels back up. But if you have symptoms, you'll be given calcium intravenously and vitamin D supplements to help with its absorption. Your doctor may treat the underlying cause of the problem if it can be identified."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Dehydration"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "Treatment for hypocalcemia may include: \n\t\t•\tIntravenous calcium and fluids \n\t\t•\tOral calcium supplements \n\t\t•\tVitamin D supplements \n\t\t•\tThiazide diuretics to decrease kidney excretion of calcium \n\t\t•\tTreatment for the underlying cause"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "These self-care strategies can help if your blood calcium levels are low: \n\t\t•\tMake sure you are getting enough calcium in your diet. Dairy products, greens, sardines, salmon, red beans, tofu, and seaweed are good sources. \n\t\t•\tAsk your doctor if you should take calcium supplements. \n\t\t•\tTake vitamin D with calcium supplements for better absorption. \n\t\t•\tDrink orange juice fortified with calcium."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Questions to Ask Your Doctor",
      "content": "\t\t•\t Could hypocalcemia or another condition be causing my symptoms? \n\t\t•\tWhat tests do I need? \n\t\t•\tIf I have hypocalcemia, what do you think caused it? \n\t\t•\tWhat treatments do I need for it? \n\t\t•\tIs there anything I can do to avoid future episodes?"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Diagnosed By",
      "content": "The doctor will do a physical exam and take blood tests to measure calcium levels. The doctor also may order other tests to check for possible causes."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "About four in every 100,000 people have hypoparathyroidism, one of the main causes of hypcalcemia."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Risk Factors",
      "content": "You are at increased risk for hypocalcemia if you have low levels of parathyroid hormone, often due to damage to the parathyroid glands during thyroid surgery. Other risk factors include being low in calcium, vitamin D, or magnesium, having some types of leukemia or other blood disorders, having tumor lysis (a complication of chemotherapy), kidney dysfunction, pancreatitis, or certain conditions that decrease calcium absorption. Certain drugs, including bisphosphonates, also put you at increased risk."

  "Hypoparathyroidism": [
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",

    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Hypoparathyroidism is a rare condition in which the parathyroid glands in the neck don't make enough of a key hormone. This leads to low calcium and high phosphorus levels in the blood, which can cause muscle twitches, seizures, cramps, tingling sensations, and developmental delays in children. Neck surgery, radiation treatments, and genetic conditions can cause hypoparathyroidism. Daily dietary supplements will help get calcium and phosphorus levels under control. Prompt treatment can help prevent lasting problems."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Hypoparathyroidism may first appear within a few weeks of a baby's birth. Others develop it later in childhood or as adults. Lifetime treatment with calcium and vitamin D supplements is standard. Getting help quickly is crucial in children; it can prevent growth delays and mental retardation. In adults, treatment can reverse many symptoms, but won't help with stunted growth, cognitive problems, cataracts and calcium deposits in the brain. Severely low levels of calcium can be life-threatening; the best treatment may be IV calcium in a hospital."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "Hypoparathyroidism is rare. One study suggests that hypoparathyroidism with an unknown cause affects 1 out of 140,000 people."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Risk Factors",
      "content": "Recent surgery in the head or neck, radiation treatment, a family history of hypoparathyroidism, low magnesium levels, excess acid in bodily fluids, DiGeorge syndrome, Addison's disease, autoimmune disorders, adrenal insufficiency. Thyroid surgery is the most frequent cause."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "Treatments include: \n\t\t•\tCalcium supplements \n\t\t•\tVitamin D supplements \n\t\t•\tDietary changes \n\t\t•\tMedications to lower phosphorus levels \n\t\t•\tIV calcium for emergencies"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "People with hypoparathyroidism need medical treatment. In some cases, changing their diets to include more foods with calcium -- such as dairy products, spinach, and kale -- and fewer foods with phosphorus -- such as sodas, eggs, and some meats -- will help."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Not getting enough calcium or getting too much phosphorus in the diet"
    },
    {

Symptoms include: muscle cramps and spasms, abdominal pain, dry hair and skin, brittle nails, cataracts, tingling fingers or toes and lips, dental problems, headaches, mood swings and depression, irregular heartbeat, weak bones, painful periods and developmental and growth delays in children. Severely low levels of calcium can be life-threatening. If you have seizures or breathing problems, go to the emergency room."
    },

Intoxication can cause euphoria, slowed reaction time, dizziness, confusion, increased heart rate, paranoia, nausea, hallucinations, increased body temperature, impaired judgment, lowered inhibitions, impaired balance, falling, impaired driving, numbness, sleeplessness, changes in emotions, increased sensitivity, anxiety, sweating, teeth clenching, reduced appetite, and violent behavior. Most people take drugs to try to feel good. But certain drugs can cause unpleasant and serious side effects, such as high blood pressure, anxiety, hallucinations, stroke, kidney failure, seizures, and coma. Stopping a drug suddenly can also cause withdrawal symptoms, including fatigue, insomnia, anxiety, nausea, vomiting, stomach cramps, and depression. If you take too much of any drug you may need emergency medical care. The illness can run in families, but people with no family history also become depressed. Often, there is no clear cause. Depression is a common but serious illness that usually doesn't go away without treatment. Counseling and/or antidepressant medication can treat depression in most people."

However, depression usually doesn't go away and often gets worse without treatment. Severe depression can make it impossible to function and cause suicidal thoughts. Treatment with antidepressants and/or talk therapy can help most people with depression. Antidepressants can take six-eight weeks to work, so it's important to be patient while finding the right treatment for you."

Caffeine dependence can lead to caffeine withdrawal, which can cause headache, fatigue, or drowsiness, depressed mood, irritability, difficulty concentrating, nausea or vomiting, and muscle pain or stiffness. Withdrawal symptoms can last from two to nine days. Most experts recommend you cut back on caffeine gradually and allow your body to adjust to the lesser amount."

      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Mitral valve prolapse is a type of heart problem. It happens when the valve between your heart's left upper and lower chambers doesn't close completely. It can sometimes cause blood to leak back into the left upper chamber. Mitral valve prolapse often runs in families and can happen with certain medical conditions. It is most common in women ages 20 to 40. Doctors aren't exactly sure what causes it. Many people don't have any symptoms, but it can cause chest pain, heart palpitations, trouble breathing with activity, dizziness, headaches, and fatigue. Often it doesn't need treatment. In rare cases, mitral valve prolapse can cause irregular heartbeats that are life-threatening."
    },
    {

  "Agoraphobia": [
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "Anxiety\nFearful"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Agoraphobia is the fear of open places or any situation where escape would be difficult. It usually occurs as a result of panic disorder. People with panic disorder have sudden attacks of terror that may include rapid heartbeat, sweating, dizziness, light-headedness, tingling hands, nausea, and chest pain. Because symptoms come on without any warning, people with panic disorder sometimes try to avoid situations or places they associate with feelings of panic. When fear of having a panic attack becomes so intense that the person rarely leaves the house, it's considered agoraphobia. Agoraphobia can make it difficult to hold down a job and makes the person very dependent on others. Depression, alcoholism, or drug abuse often occur with panic disorder. No one knows what causes panic disorders, but they tend to run in families. Antidepressants, anti-anxiety medications, and therapy help treat panic disorder and agoraphobia."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Panic attacks come on suddenly without any warning. They last about 10 minutes, but the lingering worry and dread can stay with you much longer than that. Because attacks occur without warning, most people spend a lot of time worrying about when the next attack may happen. A person with panic disorder may begin avoiding places or situations where panic attacks have occurred. For example, if you had a panic attack at the grocery store, you might avoid grocery stores. Agoraphobia develops when symptoms of panic cause a person to become housebound or only go out with someone they trust. Treating panic disorder early on can help prevent agoraphobia from developing. Medications and therapy treat panic disorder and agoraphobia."

People who are addicted to cocaine develop a tolerance to the drug, so that they need more of it to achieve the same high. Abusing cocaine increases the risk of heart attacks, strokes, digestive problems, malnutrition, respiratory problems, paranoia, hallucinations, and of contracting HIV. Eventually, the need for cocaine becomes so overwhelming that the person neglects important obligations in the effort to get and use cocaine. Postpartum depression is more severe and long-lasting. It occurs when the depression doesn't go away in two weeks or if depression develops a month or so after delivery. Postpartum depression may cause sadness, hopelessness, anxiety, loss of energy, loss of interest in things, guilt, changes in appetite, and insomnia. A woman may also have negative feelings about her baby or have little interest in her child. Some women have thoughts of harming their baby or committing suicide, although they rarely act on them. In rare cases, postpartum psychosis occurs, with hallucinations and delusions. Any woman experiencing postpartum depression symptoms should tell her doctor right away. Counseling and/or antidepressant medication effectively treat postpartum depression in women."
    },

A woman may become withdrawn, feel worthless, sad, anxious, hopeless, and have difficulty caring for her baby or herself. Postpartum depression usually doesn't go away on its own, so it's important to get help right away. Treatment with antidepressants and/or talk therapy can help most women with postpartum depression."

  "Epilepsy_(complex_partial_seizures)": [
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "\t\t•\tAnxiety\n\t\t•\tConfusion\n\t\t•\tDisorientation\n\t\t•\tBlank stare\n\t\t•\tBlackouts (memory time loss) \n\t\t•\tInvoluntary movements (picking, lip smacking etc.) \n\t\t•\tBlurred vision\n\t\t•\tLoss of consciousness\n\t\t•\tMuscle twitching (painless) \n\t\t•\tBlinking eyes\n\t\t•\tEyes rolling back\n\t\t•\tStrange smell or taste"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Epilepsy is marked by repeated seizures. There are many different types of seizures, depending on which part and how much of the brain is affected. Epilepsy that causes partial seizures begins in one local area of the brain. Partial seizures are divided into simple and complex partial seizures. During simple partial seizures, people remain fully conscious and able to respond to external stimuli. During complex partial seizures, people lose consciousness briefly. Most people with epilepsy are able to control their symptoms with the use of anti-seizure medications. Surgery can sometimes be performed to remove the damaged brain cells that cause seizures."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Complex partial seizures usually start with a blank stare. The person seems to be unaware of his or her surroundings and appears dazed, unresponsive, and clumsy. Other signs include mumbling, smacking of the lips, fidgeting, chewing, and walking in a random way. After the seizure is over, people typically have no memory of what has happened to them. Seizures do not typically cause brain damage. Between seizures, most people can lead normal lives. If your seizures are caused by an underlying condition such as low blood sugar levels, treating that condition can eliminate seizures. If there is no underlying cause, epilepsy can usually be controlled by anti-seizure medications. Surgery can also sometimes be performed to remove the damaged brain cells that cause seizures."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "If your seizures are caused by an underlying condition, your doctor will recommend treatments based on the medical problem that is causing them. For epilepsy not linked to other conditions, many anti-seizure drugs are available. These can prevent and stop seizures. Medications often need to be adjusted, especially when you first begin taking them. If medications don't control seizures, your doctor may talk to you about surgery to remove the abnormal cells in the brain that are causing seizures. Another option is vagus nerve stimulation, which delivers short bursts of electrical energy into the brain via a large nerve in the neck. In some cases, seizures may also be controlled by following a diet very high in fats and low in carbohydrates, called a ketogenic diet."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "If your seizures are caused by an underlying condition, your doctor will recommend treatments based on the medical problem that is causing them. For epilepsy not linked to other conditions, many anti-seizure drugs are available. These can prevent and stop seizures. Medications often need to be adjusted, especially when you first begin taking them. If medications don't control seizures, your doctor may talk to you about surgery to remove the abnormal cells in the brain that are causing seizures. Another option is vagus nerve stimulation, which delivers short bursts of electrical energy into the brain via a large nerve in the neck. In some cases, seizures may also be controlled by following a diet very high in fats and low in carbohydrates, called a ketogenic diet."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "Getting plenty of sleep, avoiding stress, not smoking, and avoiding alcohol may help reduce your likelihood of having a seizure. If you do experience recurring seizures, it's important for the people around you to know what to do: \n\t\t•\tStay calm \n\t\t•\tDon't move the person experiencing a seizure. \n\t\t•\tDon't try to interfere with the seizure. \n\t\t•\tDon't try to wake the person up by shouting or shaking them. \n\t\t•\tNever try to force the person's mouth open or put anything in it. \n\t\t•\tTurn the person on his or her side to allow any fluid in the mouth to come out. \n\t\t•\tPlace a pillow or something soft under the person's head. \n\t\t•\tWhen the seizure stops, watch the person for signs of confusion. Allow the person to rest or sleep.\n\nMost seizures aren't life-threatening. You don't need to call a doctor or an ambulance unless this is the first time someone has experienced a seizure or someone known to have epilepsy has a seizure that lasts longer than 5 minutes."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Drinking alcohol, smoking, not getting enough sleep, stress"
    },
    {
      "heading": "When to See Your Doctor",
      "content": "Call your doctor or go to a hospital emergency room if this is the first time you've experienced a seizure. If you've been diagnosed with epilepsy and are under treatment, you don't need immediate medical attention unless the seizure lasts more than five minutes, you have breathing difficulties, you experience persistent confusion or loss of consciousness, or you've injured yourself as a result of a seizure."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Questions to Ask Your Doctor",

    },

      "content": "Your doctor will ask you about your symptoms and do a physical examination, including tests of strength and reflexes. A test called an electroencephalogram, which measures brainwaves, is often used to diagnose epilepsy. Other tests that may be performed include MRI or CT scans."

    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Hyperthyroidism occurs when the thyroid gland produces too much thyroid hormone. The thyroid is a butterfly-shaped gland that wraps around the front of the windpipe. It controls how the body uses energy. With too much thyroid hormone, your body functions speed up. This causes weight loss, shakiness, tiredness, sensitivity to heat, insomnia, and trouble concentrating. Graves' disease, an immune system disorder, is the most common cause. Thyroid nodules, inflammation of the thyroid gland, consuming too much iodine, and taking too much thyroid hormone also can cause hyperthyroidism. Hyperthyroidism is fairly common and occurs more often in women."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Some people have hyperthyroidism and never have symptoms. Early symptoms may be mild and get worse as hyperthyroidism increases. Early symptoms include heat sensitivity, more frequent bowel movements, agitation, weight loss, and irregular periods in women. More commonly, older adults have irregular heartbeat and shortness of breath. If not treated, severe symptoms called a \"thyroid storm\" can occur, causing fever, rapid heart rate, high blood pressure, disorientation, nausea, and vomiting. If these symptoms occur, get immediate medical help. Treatment often cures the cause of hyperthyroidism and returns thyroid levels to normal."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "About 1% of people in the U.S. have hyperthyroidism."
    },

    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "If you have symptoms of hyperthyroidism, see your doctor to get an evaluation."

    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Low blood sugar happens when blood sugar, or glucose, levels in the blood drop too low. It causes shakiness, anxiety, clammy skin, irritability, headache, rapid heartbeat, and hunger. People with diabetes often get low blood sugar, which can be caused by diabetes medications, not eating enough, or exercising. Low blood sugar can be life-threatening for people with diabetes and cause seizures, coma, and even death. Certain medical conditions including cancer, medications, tumors, excess alcohol use, and hereditary conditions can cause low blood sugar in people who don't have diabetes. Some people have a condition called reactive hypoglycemia, which causes low blood sugar within four hours after eating. Treatment for low blood sugar depends on what's causing it. People with diabetes can prevent low blood sugar by checking their blood sugar levels regularly and keeping levels in a normal range. Treating underlying medical conditions can treat low blood sugar. Eating small meals every three hours can help manage reactive hypoglycemia."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Early symptoms of low blood sugar include trembling, tiredness, anxiety, hunger, and irritability. At the first sign of low blood sugar, people with diabetes should eat 15 grams of carbohydrates. Examples include 1 tablespoon of sugar or honey or one-half cup of fruit juice. If blood glucose levels remains below 70 mg/dL after 15 minutes, take another small serving of carbohydrates. Continue eating 15 grams and checking every 15 minutes until glucose levels rise above 70 mg/dL. Severe low blood sugar can cause mental confusion, extreme sleepiness, fainting, and coma. Anyone with these symptoms should get immediate medical attention. A major diabetes control study shows that people with diabetes who have a low blood sugar reaction have a 50% risk of experiencing another one within 24 hours, and a 25% risk of a third one in the following 24 hours."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "Millions of people have occasional or mild low blood sugar, but people with diabetes who take insulin and other blood sugar-lowering medications are most susceptible to significant low blood sugar."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "Treatment for low blood sugar includes: \n\t\t•\tTaking a small amount of carbohydrates \n\t\t•\tAdjusting medications \n\t\t•\tMaking changes to your diet \n\t\t•\tTreating any underlying medical conditions"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "People with diabetes can manage low blood sugar by checking blood glucose levels regularly and taking quick-fix foods as needed until blood sugar levels return to normal. People with reactive hypoglycemia can prevent low blood sugar by eating small meals three hours apart, getting regular exercise, eating a healthy diet, and not eating foods high in sugar in an empty stomach."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Taking too much insulin or diabetes medication, poor diet, not eating regular meals, over-exercising without adjusting insulin dose or diet"
    },
    {
      "heading": "When to See Your Doctor",
      "content": "If you have symptoms of low blood sugar and don't know why, call your doctor. Symptoms of low blood sugar include shakiness, anxiety, trembling, sleepiness, rapid heartbeat, headaches, sweating, clammy skin, irritability, confusion, and hunger. Very low blood sugar can cause fainting, seizures, coma, and death. Call your doctor if you have diabetes and keep having low blood sugar because you may need to adjust your treatment plan. If you develop glossitis, your tongue may swell and turn bright red. In the early stages of pellagra, you may be depressed, lethargic, and apathetic, and have trouble concentrating. As the disease advances, you may become confused, forgetful, and disoriented, have hallucinations, and go into a coma. Vitamin therapy with oral niacin or nicotinamide, a component of the B-complex vitamins, helps clear up pellagra. If you are malnourished, a high-protein diet and B-complex vitamins will also help restore your health. You may see results within a few days. Topical emollients can help clear up skin symptoms."

You may also shed pubic and eyebrow hair. If the trigger to hair loss is short-lived, this will slow over six to eight months and your hair should return to its normal fullness within a year. If the trigger for telogen effluvium is ongoing, the condition can start slower and last longer. In this form, your scalp hair thins because less hair is growing, but you may not shed much hair. In the third pattern, your hair goes through shortened growth cycles. You may shed short, thin hair fibers over a longer period of time.   "Epilepsy_(absence_or_petit_mal_seizures)": [
    {
      "heading": "Epilepsy (Absence or Petit Mal Seizures)",
      "content": "Absence seizures, petit mal seizures, seizure disorder"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "\t\t•\tBlackouts (memory time loss) \n\t\t•\tDifficulty learning new things\n\t\t•\tBlank stare\n\t\t•\tDifficulty solving problems"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Epilepsy is a brain disorder where the nerve cells in the brain signal abnormally and become overactive, causing seizures. There are many different kinds of seizures that cause a range of symptoms. Seizures can last from a few seconds to a few minutes and may occur several times a day or only occasionally. Most people experience the same type of seizure from one episode to the next. Petit mal or absence seizures cause staring spells or chewing motions. Partial or focal seizures cause small, repetitive movements from lip smacking to head tilting along with other symptoms such as hallucinations. Generalized tonic-clonic or grand mal seizures cause a person to cry out, fall down, lose consciousness, and have violent muscle contractions, which cause incontinence and convulsions. Epilepsy can affect anyone and usually has no clear cause. Stroke, brain injury, brain illness or infection, brain tumors, birth defects of the brain, certain diseases, and kidney or liver failure can also cause epilepsy. Treatment with anticonvulsive medication, surgery, or vagus nerve stimulation can help people with epilepsy manage their condition. Following a special high-fat diet may help prevent seizures in children with epilepsy."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Epilepsy can't be cured, but if it is well-controlled with medication, it sometimes goes away on its own. Some types of epilepsy that start in childhood go away with age. But many people will have epilepsy for their entire life. Medication and other treatments control seizures in nearly 80% of people with epilepsy. In people with epilepsy caused by another condition, such as a brain tumor, treating the underlying cause may stop seizures from occurring. Seizures usually don't cause permanent harm, but people with poorly controlled seizures should not drive cars or operate machinery. Prolonged seizures that last longer than five minutes require emergency treatment to prevent brain damage and death."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "Two million people in the U.S. have epilepsy."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "Epilepsy treatment may include: \n\t\t•\tAnticonvulsive medication \n\t\t•\tSpecial diets for children with epilepsy \n\t\t•\tBrain surgery \n\t\t•\tVagus nerve stimulator"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "Along with medical treatment, getting enough sleep, not drinking alcohol to excess, taking epilepsy medication as directed, and trying to avoid stress may help prevent seizures."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Alcohol, illicit drug use, stress, not getting enough sleep, not taking epilepsy medications, hormonal changes, illness, smoking, flashing lights"

      "heading": "When to See Your Doctor",
      "content": "If you have epilepsy and seizures that occur more frequently, the type of seizure changes, or you become injured from a seizure, see your doctor. After the condition subsides, people have no lasting effects, although they may not remember the time during the attack clearly. There is no treatment for transient global amnesia. It can occur without physical injury. Acute trauma symptoms are those that happen immediately after injury – such as bruises or bleeding, or shock and denial. Chronic trauma symptoms can occur long after an injury appears to have healed. They can be physical – like pain, headaches, or fatigue – or emotional – like anger, anxiety, and difficulty concentrating. Trauma usually requires medical or psychological treatment."
    },

You could need surgery, medications, hospitalization, physical therapy, and self-care for physical trauma. Sometimes symptoms of physical trauma are slightly delayed, such as head and neck pain that appears the day after an auto accident. But they can also be delayed months or even years. For example, you could have sudden or gradual pain months after a spinal cord wound. Weeks after a concussion clears up, you may have headaches, mood swings, depression, or difficulty remembering. These symptoms need treatment, but it may be different from the treatment you'd get for acute symptoms.\n\nIf you experience emotional trauma, you may feel anxious, depressed, and unable to eat or sleep. The symptoms may go away or let up after weeks or months, and you're able to resume normal activities. If they don't go away or if they resurface in stressful situations, you may have post-traumatic stress disorder (PTSD). You could have nightmares or flashbacks, feel emotionally numb or vulnerable, have attention or memory deficits, or other symptoms that affect your relationships or work.       "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Osteogenesis imperfecta is a condition that causes very fragile bones that break easily. People are born with this condition, and in most cases it is inherited from a parent. Osteogenesis imperfecta is usually caused by a defect in the gene that makes collagen. There are several types of osteogenesis imperfecta, and the symptoms can range from mild to severe. The mild type is the most common. Osteogenesis imperfecta can cause broken bones, hearing loss, a blue tint in the whites of the eyes, short height, loose joints, flat feet, and poor teeth. More severe cases can cause bowed arms and legs or a curving of the spine. In very severe cases, it can cause death."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Osteogenesis imperfecta is usually diagnosed in childhood, but very mild cases are sometimes not diagnosed until later in life. In most cases, people with osteogenesis imperfecta have a normal life span. But the most severe type usually causes death by age 1. There is no cure for osteogenesis imperfecta, but treatments can help reduce the symptoms. Bisphosphonate drugs can help increase bone strength and reduce fractures. Regular exercise can also help keep bones strong. Some people may need surgery to correct deformities or strengthen bones."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",

  "Hematoma": [
    {
      "heading": "Hematoma",
      "content": "Blood blister, blood tumor"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "\t\t•\tBruising or discoloration\n\t\t•\tTenderness to touch"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "A hematoma is a collection of blood that pools in tissue outside of a damaged blood vessel, usually after an injury. Hematomas look like a deep bruise and range in size from very small to as large as a grapefruit. Taking blood thinners--even aspirin--can increase the risk for hematomas. Most hematomas under the skin and fingernails go away on their own. However, subdural hematomas that form between the brain and skull are very dangerous and even fatal if not treated quickly."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Your body will reabsorb a small hematoma within a week or two. You'll notice it changing color from purples and blues to yellows and browns as it breaks down. A large hematoma can take months to reabsorb. The large amount of decomposing blood cells increases the risk of infection and other complications. A doctor can drain a large hematoma to reduce the risk. Subdural hematomas, which are usually picked up on CT and MRI scans taken after head trauma, need to be drained immediately by a doctor."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "There are many millions of hematomas, large and small, in the U.S. every year. Subdural hematomas in the brain are twice as common in men compared to women, possibly because men are more likely to participate in high-risk activities."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "For hematomas of the skin and muscles, treatment includes: \n\t\t•\tResting the injury \n\t\t•\tIcing the area \n\t\t•\tElevation and/or compression with an elastic bandage \n\t\t•\tTaking over-the-counter pain medication\n\nA large internal hematoma, which may become infected, should be treated by a doctor. Depending on the size and location, your doctor may drain the blood from it.\n\nFor a subdural hematoma, doctors may perform emergency surgery to relieve the pressure on the brain."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "Most small hematomas clear up on their own. However, you can drain a hematoma under a fingernail or toenail to relieve pain and pressure. Otherwise, just watch and wait as the body naturally reabsorbs the blood over time."
    },
    {

      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Brain injuries are typically caused by trauma to the head, and can cause effects that last a lifetime. Problems with memory, attention, sight, language, movement, and mood are common. Severe brain injuries often involve many areas of the brain and can lead to a variety of disabilities, including coma. Treatment depends on a person's symptoms and may be lifelong. But many people with brain injuries are able to return to their regular work and home life."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Recovery for severe brain injuries is most rapid within the first six months. After that, you may have gradual recovery for several years. Some symptoms may go away with time and treatment, and others may require continued support and assistance. Right after your injury, you may have headache, nausea, dizziness, confusion, agitation, and amnesia. Some symptoms, such as headache, may continue for several months. Later, brain injury may affect your ability to manage things when you return to regular work, home, or school. You may become easily confused, unable to concentrate, or have problems with abstract reasoning and problem solving. You may have memory loss and be unable to form or store new memories.       "content": "Carbon monoxide (CO) is a colorless and odorless gas that can cause severe and fatal poisoning. It's in gasoline and diesel exhaust, and can be produced by anything that burns fuel, natural gas heaters, fireplaces, woodstoves, and kerosene heaters. Carbon monoxide is dangerous even in small amounts, because it binds with blood cells much more rapidly and efficiently than oxygen. If heaters and stoves are not used properly, carbon monoxide can build up to dangerous levels quickly in closed spaces like a room or a garage. Carbon monoxide poisoning can cause headache, dizziness, nausea, and vomiting. But someone who is sleeping or intoxicated may not notice the symptoms."

    },

      "content": "The first sign of carbon monoxide poisoning is usually a dull headache. You also may feel dizzy, confused, or nauseated. If you are exposed to low levels of CO, you may have these symptoms over a period of time. These symptoms usually get better when you leave the area where the CO is built up. You may not realize you have carbon monoxide poisoning, because the symptoms are similar to other illnesses. Very high levels of carbon monoxide poisoning can cause brain damage or death. You can treat most cases of carbon monoxide poisoning by getting fresh air or oxygen when you first notice symptoms."

Ketoacidosis happens most often in people with type 1 diabetes. Being sick, missing an insulin dose, not eating well, heart attack, stroke, alcohol abuse, or even stress may trigger it. If you develop ketoacidosis, you may be very thirsty and nauseated. You'll need to be treated in the hospital with IV fluids containing insulin and electrolytes. With aggressive treatment, most people completely recover."

If vomiting occurs, ketoacidosis can develop very rapidly. You'll be treated in the hospital for dehydration and other symptoms with intravenous fluids and insulin. If the condition is mild, you may be sent home with instructions for monitoring and controlling your blood sugar and urinary ketone levels. They can block the sensation of pain and make you feel elated, relaxed, and sleepy. Many people take prescription narcotics to relieve pain. But if you take narcotics that aren't prescribed, or if you take them for reasons other than why they are prescribed, you may be abusing them. Abusing narcotics can cause serious health problems and can lead to addiction."

When you use narcotic drugs for a reason other than the intended medical purpose or use more than the prescribed dose, it is called abuse. Abusing narcotic drugs over a period of time can cause intense withdrawal symptoms when you try to stop. The longer you have been taking the narcotics, the more severe your withdrawal symptoms will be. Narcotics abuse can be treated with medications and behavioral counseling."

    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "A brain infection occurs when the brain, spinal cord, or membranes surrounding them become inflamed. Brain infections can be life threatening, so it's important to seek immediate medical care. There are different types of brain infections. Meningitis is an infection of the membranes surrounding the brain and spinal cord. Encephalitis is an infection of the brain itself. Viruses and bacteria commonly cause brain infections. Other less common causes include fungus, parasites, a type of protein called prions, and brain or spine abscesses. Mosquito and tick bites can spread certain types of encephalitis. A brain infection causes the brain to swell and can cause sleepiness, fever, headaches, neck pain and stiffness, nausea, fatigue, confusion, sensitivity to light, seizures, loss of consciousness, and coma. Treatment depends on the cause and ranges from self-care at home to hospitalization and IV medications."
    },
    {
      "heading": "EMERGENCY",
      "content": "Get immediate medical attention if you have symptoms of a brain infection."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "When symptoms appear, how long they last and what type of treatment depend on the cause of the brain infection. Symptoms may come on slowly and be mild and flu-like, or they can come on suddenly, causing seizures, loss of consciousness, and coma. Anyone with symptoms of a brain infection should get immediate medical attention. Treatment includes self-care at home or hospitalization. Medications may help ease symptoms and cure the infection. Recovery takes anywhere from 1 to 2 weeks to several months. Serious infections can result in damage to the nervous system and deafness."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Waiting to get medical treatment"

Only about one in every 250 people infected develops a severe case of encephalitis. JE does not occur in the United States or Europe. People mainly at risk are those who live or work in rural areas of Asia and members of the military who are deployed there.       "content": "Bipolar disorder causes extreme mood swings between depression and mania. When depressed, you may feel utterly hopeless. When manic, you may feel elated, productive, and full of energy. People around you may find you frantic, irritable, and reckless. The cycles may last for days, weeks, or months. Untreated, bipolar disorder is destructive and dangerous. Medication -- often coupled with therapy -- can help control symptoms."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Bipolar disorder most often appears in late teens and young adults. But it can develop at any age. There are several types. Bipolar disorder type I causes depression and at least one period of mania. Bipolar disorder type II causes depression that alternates with mild mania, called hypomania. Bipolar disorder can have a serious impact on family and career. During manic or hypomanic phases, you may not believe that anything is wrong. But when depressed, you may not be able to function. Bipolar disorder is a life-long condition. Often it gets better and worse at different times of your life. Most people need ongoing treatment. Untreated bipolar disorder tends to get more severe."

People with anorexia may be convinced that they are heavy and starve themselves even if they're very underweight. While more common in girls and women, boys and men develop it, too. Triggers for anorexia vary. They include a perfectionist personality, low self-esteem, emotional trauma, societal pressure to be thin, and the influence of popular media. Anorexia is hard to treat. It can be life threatening. It also can lead to complications including heart problems, bone loss, and kidney problems. Therapy and behavior changes can help."
    },

Experts suggest a slow weight gain of 1 pound to 3 pounds a week until someone has reached a normal weight. Family support is crucial. Some people benefit from a stay in the hospital. People who develop anorexia at a young age are more likely to have a full recovery. Even after successful treatment, anorexia can come back."

    {
      "heading": "Asperger Syndrome",
      "content": "Asperger's syndrome, Asperger's disorder, Asperger syndrome"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "\t\t•\tCompulsive behavior\n\t\t•\tLoss of coordination\n\t\t•\tDislikes change in daily routine\n\t\t•\tDifficulty learning new things\n\t\t•\tDifficulty solving problems\n\t\t•\tSensitive to noise\n\t\t•\tDevelopmental delays\n\t\t•\tUnusual facial expression\n\t\t•\tSocially withdrawn"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Asperger's syndrome is one of a group of childhood developmental conditions known as \"autism spectrum disorders.\" It is most common in boys. Children with Asperger's syndrome usually have normal to superior intelligence. Unlike children with autism, they don't have delayed cognitive or language development. But they can have odd speech patterns, great difficulties with socializing and communication, and obsessive-compulsive traits. People are born with Asperger syndrome, and research suggests that it may run in families. Though the social impairments of Asperger's syndrome are believed to be lifelong, educational and social support helps many children go on to lead independent lives."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "You may sense that there is something different about your child at around age 3. The first thing you notice may be delays in motor development – such as late crawling or walking. This could evolve into trouble pedaling, catching, or climbing. Obsessive interest in a single topic or object is one of the main characteristics of Asperger's syndrome. For example, a child may spend hours counting passing cars or will only talk about trains. Your child will have difficulty with social interaction, and may be rigid and resist change. Your child may be exceptionally skilled in some areas, such as with numbers, math, computers, or music. A child or adolescent psychiatrist can diagnose your child and work with you to develop an effective program to help him or her. There is no specific way to deal with Asperger's syndrome, but most treatment combines psychotherapy, special education, behavior modification, and support for parents. Medication is sometimes recommended. With good, early help, many children are able to cope with disabilities caused by Asperger's syndrome and go on to college and careers. Your child may continue to need support throughout life to deal with the challenges of social and personal relationships."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "Estimates of how common Asperger's syndrome is in the U.S. vary widely. Some studies say it affects 1 in 250 children; others say 1 in 1,000 children."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Risk Factors",
      "content": "Males are at higher risk than females."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "Treatment for Asperger's syndrome may include: \n\t\t•\tSocial skills training – a form of group therapy to help children with Asperger's syndrome learn how to interact with other children \n\t\t•\tCognitive behavioral therapy – \"talk therapy\" to help children manage their emotions and reduce obsessive interests and routines \n\t\t•\tOccupational or physical therapy for poor motor coordination \n\t\t•\tSpeech/language therapy \n\t\t•\tParent training and support \n\t\t•\tMedication if your child has conditions like anxiety, anger, and inattention"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "These home strategies can help a child with Asperger's syndrome: \n\t\t•\tUse routines, minimize change, and prepare for transitions. \n\t\t•\tTalk in a slow, clear language. \n\t\t•\tUse your child's special interests to teach new concepts when possible. \n\t\t•\tProvide breaks from social situations when your child needs them."

    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Aspirin poisoning can come on suddenly after taking a high dose of aspirin or gradually after taking lower doses for several weeks. You may have nausea, vomiting, ringing in your ears, drowsiness, confusion, and rapid breathing. Aspirin poisoning is usually a medical emergency because it can cause unconsciousness or death. It's a common cause of poisoning in children but less so because of child-proof packaging and public education. Intentional aspirin poisoning, such as in an attempted suicide, causes most sudden aspirin poisoning. You can develop gradual poisoning by unknowingly taking a combination of medications that contain aspirin. Activated charcoal, and in severe cases, hemodialysis, are used to treat it. With proper treatment, most people survive."
    },
    {
      "heading": "EMERGENCY",
      "content": "If you suspect that you or someone else has taken too much aspirin but there are no symptoms, call poison control at (800) 222-1222 or your local poison control center."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "If you have sudden, or acute, poisoning from aspirin (salicylate), you'll probably first have nausea and vomiting, ringing in your ears, rapid breathing, and sweating. You may not have symptoms of severe poisoning for a few days. Then you may be lightheaded, drowsy, hyperactive, or confused or have fever, seizures, kidney failure, or difficulty breathing. If you have gradual aspirin poisoning, your symptoms will develop over days or weeks. You may feel drowsy and confused or have hallucinations. Eventually, you could have dehydration, seizures, and brain swelling. For all but the most minor symptoms of aspirin poisoning, you'll need emergency treatment in a hospital. The doctors will give you activated charcoal and intravenous fluids and may pump your stomach (gastric lavage). You may need a ventilator to help you breathe or medications for complications such as seizures. If your condition worsens, you could need dialysis -- a procedure that uses an artificial kidney to filter poisons out of your blood. After emergency treatment, you'll probably be hospitalized for further treatment and monitoring. If the overdose was intentional, you'll see a mental health specialist. If the dose of aspirin was not too high and you get proper treatment, you will most likely recover."
    },

      "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Taking more salicylate in any form"
    },

    },

  "Atrial_fibrillation": [
    {
      "heading": "Atrial Fibrillation",
      "content": "Atrial arrhythmia, auricular fibrillation, a. fib"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "\t\t•\tConfusion\n\t\t•\tDizziness\n\t\t•\tFeeling faint\n\t\t•\tLightheadedness\n\t\t•\tPressure or heaviness\n\t\t•\tFainting\n\t\t•\tPain or discomfort\n\t\t•\tFatigue\n\t\t•\tIrregular heartbeat\n\t\t•\tPalpitations (fluttering in chest) \n\t\t•\tShortness of breath\n\t\t•\tWeakness (generalized)"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Atrial fibrillation is the most common problem with the heart's rhythm. It is also the main cause of heart failure and a major risk for stroke. The heart has four chambers. The two upper chambers are called atria. Blood enters these chambers, which then contract to fill the lower chambers, called ventricles. Then the lower chambers contract and pump blood to the rest of the body. With a normal heartbeat, contractions follow in order, and both the upper and lower chambers work at the same pace. With fibrillation, though, the upper chambers beat much faster than the lower ones. So the ventricles don't fill completely and the heart has to work harder to get blood to the rest of the body."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "It's possible to have atrial fibrillation and not notice any symptoms. If you do have symptoms, you may first notice your pulse is irregular or hard to find because it's so weak. You also may feel tired and weak. And you may have the sensation you can feel your heart racing. But it's possible you may go for months before a doctor notices a problem during a physical exam. The longer it goes untreated, the more likely you are to notice symptoms of heart failure. These can include shortness of breath, swelling ankles, or a chronic cough with foamy mucus. Strokes are also a potential problem. So you may need to take blood thinners on a regular basis to prevent them. Often, the treatment to regulate the heartbeat works at first, but the fibrillation returns later."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "A half-million new cases of atrial fibrillation are diagnosed each year in the U.S."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "Treatment depends on how severe your symptoms are and whether or not you already have heart disease.\n\nIf there is something causing the fibrillation, the first step is to address it. This could mean making lifestyle changes such as cutting out alcohol or caffeine and eating a heart-healthy diet. It also could mean treating a medical condition such as an overactive thyroid. \n\nThe doctor may prescribe heart rhythm medications such as calcium channel blockers. \n\nYou may need to take a blood thinner such as warfarin to reduce your risk of stroke. \n\nIn some cases, doctors use electricity to shock the heart back into a normal rhythm, a process called cardioversion. \n\nSurgery may be an option if other treatments do not work."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Certain medications, hyperthyroidism, too much thyroid, too much caffeine, alcohol, and magnesium deficiency"
    },
    {
      "heading": "When to See Your Doctor",
      "content": "If you notice a weak, irregular pulse and have been feeling tired and weak, see your doctor for an evaluation. Call 911 or emergency services if you have severe chest pain, have signs of a stroke, or feel faint and have an irregular heartbeat."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Questions to Ask Your Doctor",
      "content": "\t\t•\tDo I have heart failure? \n\t\t•\tWhat tests will I need to find the problem with my heart? \n\t\t•\tHow are the tests performed? \n\t\t•\tWhat are my treatment and medication options? \n\t\t•\tWill I need to take blood thinners?"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Diagnosed By",
      "content": "Atrial fibrillation is often diagnosed with a physical exam and an electrocardiogram (EKG). Some patients' heartbeats may stay regular during an EKG. So they wear a Holter monitor that records the heart's rhythm over a 24-hour period. Others wear a monitor they can activate when they feel their heartbeat become irregular. Ultrasound, or echocardiogram, is sometimes used to look for conditions that may accompany atrial fibrillation."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Risk Factors",
      "content": "History of Graves' disease or hyperthyroidism, advanced age, alcoholism, family history of irregular heartbeats, coronary artery heart disease, abnormal heart valves"
    }
  ],

  "Dehydration_(Adult)": [
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "\t\t•\tConfusion\n\t\t•\tDecreased urination\n\t\t•\tDry mouth\n\t\t•\tFeeling faint\n\t\t•\tPale skin\n\t\t•\tThick saliva or mucus\n\t\t•\tMuscle cramps or spasms (painful) \n\t\t•\tDecreased sweating\n\t\t•\tPounding heart (pulse) \n\t\t•\tSunken eyes\n\t\t•\tConstipation\n\t\t•\tDizziness\n\t\t•\tFatigue\n\t\t•\tIncreased thirst\n\t\t•\tRapid heart rate (pulse) \n\t\t•\tFainting\n\t\t•\tDrinking excessive fluids\n\t\t•\tLow blood pressure\n\t\t•\tSwollen tongue\n\t\t•\tWeakness (generalized) \n\t\t•\t"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Dehydration happens when your body loses more water than it takes in. You naturally lose water every day, though urine, sweat, and even breathing. Normally, the water in what you drink and eat makes up for the fluid you've lost. Dehydration becomes a problem when the loss becomes excessive – from vomiting or diarrhea, for example -- and you can't get enough fluid to replace it. Or it can happen because of excessive sweating from working out on a hot day without adequate access to water. Not getting enough fluid can cause low blood pressure, weakness, dizziness, fatigue, and even nausea. The larger the deficit of fluid, the greater the risk is for serious complications. Dehydration can lead to such problems as kidney failure, confusion, coma, or shock."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Your body needs between 2 liters and 3 liters of fluid every day. Not getting that much or needing more because of illness or increased physical activity can lead to mild dehydration. You can treat mild dehydration by sipping water or other beverages, or by sucking on ice cubes or frozen ice pops. If the loss of fluid is severe, and the person has not gotten or can't get enough fluid to replace it, they may need IV fluids."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "Dehydration is common, even in hospital settings. The people most vulnerable to dehydration are children and people older than age 60."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "Treatment for dehydration depends on the age of the person and how severe the dehydration is. The primary treatment is to replace the fluids that have been lost. With mild to moderate dehydration that often can be done by drinking more fluids. If the case is severe, it may be necessary to give IV fluids. If dehydration is the result of an illness, the doctor will also treat the illness or adjust medications to control the symptoms and reduce the amount of fluid that is being lost."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "For mild dehydration, sip water, suck ice chips, or drink sports drinks such as Gatorade. Taking medication to control diarrhea, vomiting, or fever will help slow the loss of fluid associated with an illness."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Working out, hot environment, lack of water, caffeine, alcohol, and diuretics"
    },

    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "An intracranial hematoma happens when a head injury causes blood to collect within the brain or between your brain and skull. Acute subdural hematomas cause rapid bleeding in the brain. They are usually the result of a severe injury to the head. Subacute subdural hematomas can cause symptoms that appear days or weeks after an injury. Chronic subdural hematomas develop within the brain over weeks or months. They typically occur in alcoholics and among older people. An epidural hematoma happens when a blood vessel ruptures between your brain and skull, and usually causes immediate symptoms. Intracerebral hematomas occur within the brain. Any intracranial hematoma can be a potentially life-threatening condition and require emergency care."
    },
    {
      "heading": "EMERGENCY",
      "content": "Intracranial epidural hematomas are the most serious complications of head injury. They require immediate diagnosis and treatment to prevent brain damage or death. If you or someone you are with has symptoms of intracranial hematoma or has had a head injury and lost consciousness, call 911 or go to an emergency room immediately."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Some intracranial hematomas can be minor and heal themselves. Others can be life-threatening and need emergency surgery. You should always see a doctor after a head injury. Prognosis depends on many things, including the extent of the injury, the size of the hematoma, your age, and your overall health. Recovery from serious intracranial hematomas may take many months."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Medications that thin the blood, including aspirin, can increase the risk of bleeding in the brain."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "Drugs may be used to reduce pressure within the brain and to lower fever, as well as to prevent convulsions. You may need surgery to relieve the pressure caused by bleeding in the brain. This is done by creating a small hole in the skull to drain fluid. In some cases, part of the skull may have to be removed to ease pressure."
    },
    {
      "heading": "When to See Your Doctor",
      "content": "To be safe, see a doctor after any head injury. Seek immediate medical care for the symptoms of intracranial hematoma or after a head injury that causes loss of consciousness."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Questions to Ask Your Doctor",
      "content": "\t\t•\tHow severe is the bleeding in my brain? \n\t\t•\tWill the pressure go away on its own? \n\t\t•\tHas my brain been damaged? \n\t\t•\tWhat kinds of drugs will help? \n\t\t•\tWill you need to operate? \n\t\t•\tWill I recover completely? \n\t\t•\tCan I do anything to avoid another intracranial hematoma?"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Diagnosed By",
      "content": "CT and/or MRI scans are used to detect intracranial hematomas. Bloods tests also may be used to assess a person's condition."

Usually this means treating the cause of the failure, but it can include dialysis to remove toxins and excess fluid from the body. You may have no symptoms of kidney failure or be quite ill. It can be fatal, but many people make a full recovery."
    },
    {

    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Kidney failure is most common in people who already are hospitalized. The first symptom is often swelling in the ankles, feet, face, and hands. You also may have dark-colored urine or little urine, fatigue, confusion, or nausea. You may need a long hospital stay to care for acute kidney failure. Your doctors will treat the underlying illness or injury and give you medications to prevent complications from dehydration, accumulating wastes, or excess water in your body. Treating the underlying cause usually clears up the problem. If it doesn't, you'll need hemodialysis to take over kidney function while your kidneys heal. Probably a few times a week, you'll be hooked up to a dialysis machine that circulates your blood through an artificial kidney, which filters out waste and toxins and then returns the blood to your body. If your kidneys are too badly damaged to recover, you may have chronic kidney failure and need routine dialysis or a kidney transplant."
    },

The disease also can spread from mother to baby and through blood transfusions. The disease has two phases. The acute stage lasts a few weeks or months and may have few or mild symptoms, including fever, ill feeling, and swelling around one eye or near the insect bite. Once symptoms go away, it may be years before other, more serious health problems develop if the disease is not treated, including heart or intestinal problems. Treatment, especially at an early stage, can cure Chagas disease."

Thirty percent of people with chronic disease develop heart problems such as heart failure or heart rhythm problems. Ten percent develop digestive or other problems. Treatment, especially at an early stage, can cure Chagas disease."

You can get cyanide poisoning from eating or drinking foods that contain it, touching water or soil that contains it, or breathing it in. Cyanide poisoning is a medical emergency.       "content": "Lead poisoning is a serious condition that results from exposure to lead, a toxic metal that used to be common in paint and gasoline. Other sources of lead are pipes, soil and dust, storage batteries, and some toys, furniture, or cosmetics from other countries. Lead poisoning may cause trouble sleeping, abdominal cramping, or anemia. Lead can damage almost all parts of the body, and cause problems such as reduced IQ and slowed body growth. Young children are at increased risk for lead poisoning, because their nervous systems are more sensitive to lead. They also are more likely to put unwashed items into their mouths, and their small size leads to higher lead levels. The most important thing is to remove all sources of lead. In severe cases, chelation therapy can help remove lead from the body."
    },

      "content": "Seek immediate medical care if you or your child has symptoms of high lead levels. This may include vomiting, staggering, muscle weakness, seizures, or coma."

This may include decreased bone and muscle growth, speech and language problems, damage to the nervous system and kidneys, and developmental delays. If needed, treatment can help lower lead levels and minimize its effects. Depending on severity and length of exposure, recovery can take months to years."
