      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Typhoid fever is a life-threatening illness caused by Salmonella bacteria. Fever, general aches and pains, headache, weakness, and lack of appetite are common symptoms. Until typhoid fever is treated, it is highly contagious. You can get it from contaminated food or water or close contact with someone who has it. It occurs most often in developing countries with poor sanitation. It is rare in the U.S. Most cases are due to international travel. Untreated, typhoid fever can be fatal. Antibiotics clear it up, but a small number of people become carriers and need further treatment. A vaccine can also help prevent typhoid fever."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "You'll begin to have typhoid fever symptoms one or two weeks after the Salmonella typhi bacterium enters your body. You'll probably have a fever as high as 104 degrees Fahrenheit for several days. You may also feel achy, have severe headaches and abdominal pain, and have constipation or diarrhea. Loss of appetite, lethargy, and a rash of flat, rose-colored spots may also occur. If you don't receive treatment, you could develop intestinal bleeding or perforation after two or three weeks, the fever could continue for months, and you could die. With antibiotics, your symptoms will probably improve within a couple of days and you'll recover within a week to 10 days. If the illness subsides but you become a carrier, you may need to continue taking antibiotics. And if you work handling food or with small children, you probably won't be able to go back to work until you no longer carry the disease. Rarely, carriers need surgery to remove the gall bladder where Salmonella bacteria multiply."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Risk Factors",

    },

People with damaged heart valves, artificial heart valves, or other heart problems are most at risk for endocarditis, which can cause congestive heart failure and stroke. Children with developmental disabilities such as mental retardation or autism are more likely to develop the condition. Not getting enough iron or other nutrients also may cause pica. People with pica may crave and eat non-food items such as dirt, feces, string, paint chips, laundry detergent, and coffee grounds. Pica can be harmless or quite serious. Bowel obstruction, lead poisoning, parasite infection, or viral illness can occur, depending on what items are eaten. The condition often goes away on its own in a few months. In some people, pica can be a lifelong problem, especially if it occurs along with a developmental disability. Treatment may involve behavioral therapy, medication, and teaching parents how to prevent non-food eating in children."

Lice dig into the skin and feed on blood, causing intense itching and skin irritation. Lice are most common in children and spread easily from person to person from close contact. They can also be spread by using combs, brushes, bedding, or towels from an infected person. While they're small, you can see white lice eggs, called nits, in the hair around the neck and ears. Nits look like dandruff but stay firmly attached to the hair. Lice are harder to spot unless there are a lot of them. Over-the-counter or prescription shampoos and combing hair with a special nit comb will treat head lice."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Some people can have lice and not know it. For others, itching and irritation occur quickly. Usually, the small, white eggs can easily be seen around the neck and ears by parting the hair. Adult lice are harder to spot. Lice can live for up to 40 days on a human but will die in 1 to 2 days if they fall off. Eggs last for 2 weeks, and once they hatch, the cycle can start again. So it's important to treat head lice as soon as it's spotted. Having lice does not mean that a person has poor hygiene or lives in poverty. Lice don't discriminate and will infect any one regardless of social or economic status. Using OTC or prescription shampoos or lotions, removing nits by hand, and cleaning bedding and clothing will cure head lice."

It's more common in women, African-Americans, and Asian-Americans. Other types of lupus -- such as cutaneous lupus erythematosus -- only affect the skin.   "Premenstrual_dysphoric_disorder_(PMDD)": [
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "n\t\t•\tDepressed mood \n\t\t•\tFits of rage"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Premenstrual dysphoric disorder (PMDD) is a severe form of premenstrual syndrome, or PMS. It occurs just prior to menstruation and goes away during your period. It causes mood swings such as depression, anger, irritability, and tension that are significant enough to interfere with work, school, relationships, and social life. PMDD affects a small percentage of women. The causes are not fully known, but stress, hormonal changes, vulnerability in brain chemistry, and fluctuation of the brain chemical serotonin may play a part. Medications, counseling, nutritional strategies, and lifestyle changes can help improve symptoms."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Premenstrual dysphoric disorder involves a worsening of mood that disrupts your daily life. During the days before your period, you may have anger that seems out of control or you feel overwhelmed or hopeless. You may lose interest in social activities or work and have trouble concentrating. Physical symptoms such as bloating and headache may also occur. When your period starts, the symptoms begin to decrease. If the symptoms appear during other times in your cycle, PMMD is probably not the cause. If your PMDD is mild, you may be able to manage it with regular exercise, stress-reduction techniques, and changes in diet. If your symptoms don't improve or if you have severe symptoms, your doctor may suggest an antidepressant, usually a serotonin reuptake inhibitor (SSRI). Psychological counseling and other medications, including hormonal therapies, can also ease PMDD symptoms. Give your treatment two or three menstrual cycles to see if your symptoms improve before talking with your doctor about a different approach."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "Premenstrual dysphoric disorder affects between 2% and 10% of women during their reproductive years."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "Treatment for premenstrual dysphoric disorder may include: \n\t\t•\tDietary changes, such as eating frequent, small meals rich in complex carbohydrates and low in salt, fat, and caffeine \n\t\t•\tLimiting alcohol \n\t\t•\tQuitting smoking \n\t\t•\tRegular exercise and sleep \n\t\t•\tNutritional supplements such as B6, E, calcium, and magnesium \n\t\t•\tStress management, such as learning stress-reduction techniques \n\t\t•\t\"Talk therapy,\" such as cognitive behavioral therapy or interpersonal psychotherapy \n\t\t•\tAntidepressants, usually SSRIs, taken during all or part of the month, to help emotional and physical symptoms \n\t\t•\tHormonal therapy, including birth control pills in some cases \n\t\t•\tMedications to treat symptoms such as headaches and water retention"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "These self-care strategies may help premenstrual dysphoric disorder: \n\t\t•\tExercise at least 3 times a week. \n\t\t•\tEat a balanced diet, especially fruits, vegetables, and whole grains. \n\t\t•\tGet plenty of rest. \n\t\t•\tDuring your PMDD days, keep your schedule light and ask for extra help with tasks such as housekeeping and childcare. \n\t\t•\tTo relax, try yoga, meditation, massage, or stress-reduction exercises. \n\t\t•\tTake an over-the-counter medication such as ibuprofen for PMDD pain or headache. \n\t\t•\tLimit sugar, salt, alcohol, and caffeine in your diet. \n\t\t•\tKeep a journal of your symptoms for 2 months or more. Water retention, which can cause up to 3 pounds of weight gain, and migraines are both common symptoms. It isn't clear what causes PMS. But one factor seems to be changes in hormones that occur during the menstrual cycle. A severe form of PMS, marked by such symptoms as major depression and angry outbursts at oneself or at others, is called \"premenstrual dysphoric disorder\" or PMDD."

      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Seasonal depression, or SAD, is depression that occurs every year during a certain season. SAD can cause problems ranging from sadness and sleep problems to anxiety and moodiness. Doctors aren't exactly sure what causes SAD, but they think it may have to do with light, hormones, and body temperature. More women than men have SAD, and the symptoms usually begin during the teen or young adult years. SAD is treated with light therapy, antidepressants, and psychotherapy."

Sometimes, it is hard to know if a drug is causing the mood disorder. Many people who have a mood disorder, such as depression or mania, also use alcohol or drugs.       "content": "n\t\t•\tDifficult to wake from sleep \n\t\t•\tHeadache \n\t\t•\tProlonged breathing pauses \n\t\t•\tDisorientation \n\t\t•\tDifficulty staying asleep \n\t\t•\tRestless (tossing and turning) sleep \n\t\t•\tEpisodes of not breathing during sleep \n\t\t•\tFatigue \n\t\t•\tMemory problems \n\t\t•\tSnoring \n\t\t•\tDrowsiness \n\t\t•\tDifficulty staying awake during day \n\t\t•\tOverweight"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "People with sleep apnea stop and restart breathing multiple times while sleeping. Episodes of not breathing can last a few seconds to a few minutes. It may happen 5 to 30 times or more an hour. In most cases, breathing stops when the airway collapses, blocking the flow of air into and out of the lungs. In a rarer form, the episodes result from a problem with how the brain controls breathing. Sleep apnea causes you to wake frequently throughout the night, even though you may have no memory in the morning of being restless. Although it can be managed with proper treatment, sleep apnea often goes undetected. When not treated, it raises the risk of heart disease and other serious health problems."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Sleep apnea develops slowly. People may have it for years before it's even suspected. Some may notice symptoms such as being tired, but a person's bed partner is often the first to raise concerns. Your partner may already have remarked on your snoring or \"funny\" breathing and urged you to see a doctor. Because obesity is a major risk factor, your doctor may want you to lose weight along with making other lifestyle changes. Sleep apnea increases the risk for high blood pressure, heart failure, diabetes, and heart rhythm problems. It can make asthma worse, lead to pregnancy complications, and contribute to eye problems such as glaucoma. You'll need regular screenings and will have to work closely with your doctor to manage your health. Once the apnea improves, though, you should see positive improvements in the overall quality of your life."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "More than 12 million people in the U.S. have sleep apnea."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "Treatment for sleep apnea may include:\n\t\t•\tLifestyle changes such as losing weight, not smoking, avoiding alcohol and certain medications, improving sleeping habits \n\t\t•\tContinuous positive air pressure, which uses a small device and a face mask to provide a steady stream of air to keep your throat open while you sleep \n\t\t•\tDental devices that either hold the lower jaw in place so it doesn't block the air flow or hold the tongue in place to keep it from blocking the airway \n\t\t•\tMedication to treat the sleepiness symptom \n\t\t•\tSurgery to remove tissue and widen the airway"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "You can find some relief from symptoms by doing any or all of the following: \n\t\t•\tLosing weight \n\t\t•\tNot smoking \n\t\t•\tNot drinking alcohol \n\t\t•\tExercising regularly \n\t\t•\tSleeping on your side"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Smoking, drinking alcohol, weight gain, allergies that cause nasal congestion, certain medications like sleeping pills or sedatives -- especially before bed"
    },
    {
      "heading": "When to See Your Doctor",
      "content": "See your doctor if you have any symptoms of sleep apnea, including daytime sleepiness, fatigue, depression, mood swings, and trouble concentrating. Also see your doctor if your partner expresses concern about your snoring or tells you there are times at night when you don't breathe."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Questions to Ask Your Doctor",

    },
    {
      "heading": "Diagnosed By",
      "content": "Your doctor will take a medical and sleep history and do a physical exam to determine if something else might be causing your symptoms. However, the doctor will not be able to diagnose sleep apnea without a sleep study. You'll likely be referred to an overnight sleep center where your sleep will be monitored electronically."
    },

      "heading": "Sleep Deprivation",

    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Sleep deprivation occurs when the lack of restful sleep is severe enough to compromise basic body functions and thinking. Different people need different amounts of sleep. But on average, adults need 7 to 9 hours each night to avoid sleep deprivation. People become sleep deprived for many reasons. Some have sleep apnea, restless leg syndrome, or insomnia. Others work night shifts, and others simply try to get by on little sleep. Whatever the reason, sleep deprivation can cause daytime sleepiness, fatigue, lack of concentration, irritability, erection problems, and depression. It also is linked to weight gain and may increase the risk of heart disease."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "One night of inadequate sleep usually causes only fatigue and spaciness. If you go 24 to 48 hours without sleep, job performance and other mental functions quickly deteriorate. In such cases, driving and running machinery is risky because there may be mental lapses. Falling asleep suddenly is a sign of sleep deprivation and can be very dangerous. Chronic sleep deprivation can cause depression and other long-term problems such as erectile dysfunction and high blood pressure and may contribute to heart disease."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "An estimated 50 to 70 million Americans regularly don't get enough sleep."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "Good sleep habits can help you avoid sleep deprivation. See tips in the Self-Care section below.     },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "If you are not getting enough sleep, these tips can help improve your sleep: \n\t\t•\tGo to bed and get up at the same time every day. \n\t\t•\tDon't use alcohol, caffeine, or nicotine before going to bed. \n\t\t•\tUse the bedroom just for sleeping and sex. \n\t\t•\tDon't take daytime naps. \n\t\t•\tMake sure your bedroom is not too hot or too cold and that it's dark, quiet, and relaxing."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Certain medications, excess alcohol, smoking, too much caffeine, drug abuse"
    },
    {
      "heading": "When to See Your Doctor",
      "content": "Talk with your doctor if you routinely feel tired or fatigued during the day or regularly have trouble sleeping at night. Also call the doctor if your bed partner tells you that you snore and routinely stop breathing while you sleep."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Questions to Ask Your Doctor",
      "content": "n\t\t•\tHow can I get a good night's sleep? \n\t\t•\tAre there treatment options that can help me sleep better? \n\t\t•\tAre there any tests that I need? \n\t\t•\tAre there any natural remedies I might use to help me sleep better?"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Diagnosed By",
      "content": "Your doctor will take a medical history, do a physical exam, and ask you questions about your sleeping patterns and habits. For a sleep study, a technician will monitor your sleep pattern, including any breathing problems, how often you wake up during the night, and how your legs move during sleep. The results will be analyzed by a specialist to see if you have a medical problem such as sleep apnea or restless leg syndrome."

They may sleep for up to 18 hours a day without feeling refreshed afterward, and have trouble waking up from a long sleep. Sometimes, hypersomnia has no clear cause. Drug and alcohol abuse and conditions such as sleep apnea, restless legs syndrome, and depression are also associated with hypersomnia. Medications and lifestyle changes can help."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Often, doctors can't find a cause for hypersomnia. It tends to first appear in teens and young adults. It may develop slowly with symptoms lasting for months or years. In that case, treating the underlying condition may clear up the symptoms. Hypersomnia is not dangerous itself, but it can have a severe impact on a person's life. It also increases the risks of auto accidents."
    },

      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Postconcussive syndrome occurs when the symptoms of a concussion or other traumatic brain injury continue to occur. Headaches, dizziness, anxiety, irritability, mood swings, trouble concentrating or remembering, and other symptoms may show up right away or not for days or weeks after the injury. What causes the condition is unclear, but it happens more often after concussions from car accidents and assaults than from sports. There is no specific treatment for postconcussive syndrome, so doctors generally treat the symptoms."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Some symptoms of postconcussive syndrome may go away quickly and others – like fatigue – may be more difficult to clear up. It's a good idea to see a neurologist for postconcussive syndrome, because the condition may be difficult to diagnose. How you treat it depends on your symptoms. Your doctor may prescribe medication to relieve headaches. Biofeedback can improve attention and memory. For anxiety, depression, or other emotional problems caused by the condition, it may help to take an antidepressant or see a therapist who is experienced with brain injuries. About half of people with postconcussive syndrome have symptoms for up to three months, and about one in 10 people still have symptoms at one year."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "Each year, about 1.4 million people in the U.S. suffer traumatic brain injury, and about 40% of those develop postconcussive syndrome."

    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "No one with postconcussive syndrome should participate in contact sports until symptoms have resolved.       "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Exertion, stress, alcohol"

    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Anemia is a condition in which the body doesn't have enough red blood cells to carry oxygen to the tissues. Anemia can cause fatigue, pale skin, weakness, dizziness, headache, chest pain, and shortness of breath. There are many types of anemia and many causes, including blood loss and vitamin deficiencies. Pregnancy, chronic diseases, cancer, and autoimmune disorders can also cause anemia. Depending on the type of anemia you have, there are several different treatments."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "The type of anemia symptoms you have and how severe your symptoms are depends on the cause of your anemia. Iron-deficiency anemia is the most common type of anemia. It is more common in women, caused by blood loss during menstrual periods. Blood loss because of cancer or ulcers can also cause this type of anemia. Vitamin-deficiency anemia is another type caused by low levels of B-12 and folate vitamins. This type causes muscle weakness, dizziness, shortness of breath, and diarrhea. The symptoms may increase as the vitamin deficiency gets worse. Aplastic anemia is a type of anemia that occurs when bone marrow can't produce new red blood cells. Almost all types of anemia get better with treatment."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "Anemia caused by iron deficiency is common in women and children. Twelve percent of women aged 12 to 49 have iron deficiency anemia. Seven percent of children aged 1 to 2 have it."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "The treatment for anemia depends on what's causing the anemia. Iron-deficiency anemia and vitamin-deficiency anemia are usually treated with iron and vitamin supplements and changes in the diet. You may need blood transfusions, surgery, or medications for other types of anemia."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "If your anemia is caused by an iron or vitamin deficiency, eating a healthy diet rich in iron and vitamins can help improve symptoms."

    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Anemia occurs when a person has too few red blood cells. These cells carry oxygen and other nutrients throughout the body. Signs of anemia can include weakness, tiredness, difficulty breathing, dizziness, and other symptoms. Anemia of chronic disease (ACD) is caused by certain inflammatory or infectious diseases, such as autoimmune disorders, cancer, kidney disease, cirrhosis, and long-term infections such as HIV and hepatitis B. ACD is likely a protective reaction the body has to the underlying disease. All living cells, including bacteria and even cancer cells, need iron. ACD limits the amount of iron that's available for whatever agent is causing the disease. Treatment for ACD consists of treating the underlying condition that is causing it."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Anemia of chronic disease develops slowly in response to a chronic inflammatory or infectious disease or cancer. When symptoms appear, they are usually mild. The condition usually does not get worse. Severe cases requiring transfusion or other treatments are very rare. ACD usually improves or goes away on its own when the disease that's causing it is treated."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "Anemia of chronic disease is the second most common form of anemia and the most common form of anemia in patients in the hospital."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "Anemia of chronic disease is usually mild and the disease generally requires no treatment. When the underlying condition that's causing it is treated, ACD usually improves or goes away on its own. A very severe case may require a blood transfusion, but that is rare. Iron supplements and vitamins, which may be used to treat other types of anemia, are not effective. In fact, taking iron supplements could cause life-threatening complications."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "For ACD it's important to follow the treatment plan for the condition that is causing it. Trying to treat the anemia on your own with iron supplements could be dangerous. Do not take any over-the-counter supplements without discussing it with your doctor first."
    },
    {
      "heading": "When to See Your Doctor",
      "content": "See your doctor if you have an infectious or inflammatory disease and develop any symptoms of anemia. Symptoms of anemia can include pale complexion, dizziness, fatigue, rapid heartbeat, headache, and shortness of breath. It's also important to keep all your doctor appointments for treating the underlying disease.\n\nIf you have ACD accompanied by chest pain, call 911 or seek immediate medical attention."

  "Insomnia": [
    {
      "heading": "Insomnia",
      "content": "Difficulty sleeping, sleeplessness"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "n\t\t•\tDifficulty concentrating \n\t\t•\tDifficulty falling asleep \n\t\t•\tDifficulty staying awake during day \n\t\t•\tDifficulty sleeping \n\t\t•\tDifficulty staying asleep"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Insomnia is the inability to get to sleep or stay asleep at night. Anyone at any age can experience insomnia. Drinking alcohol or caffeine, stress, medications, an uncomfortable sleep environment, and work schedule changes can lead to short-term insomnia. Chronic insomnia often results from mental health problems and can be a sign of depression. Medical conditions such as COPD or sleep apnea can also cause chronic sleep problems. Most of the time, good sleep habits, such as keeping a regular sleep schedule and not consuming caffeine before bed, can resolve the problem. Treating underlying medical conditions can also improve sleep."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Many people have insomnia for a short while due to stress, jet lag, medication side-effects, pain from an injury, or some other problem. But some people suffer from chronic insomnia, which may be harder to treat. If a medical condition, such as COPD or heart failure, makes sleeping difficult, talk with your doctor about ways to ease night-time symptoms so you can get enough sleep. Sleep problems are often associated with certain types of mental illness, including depression, anxiety, and bipolar disorder. If lack of sleep makes it hard to function during the day or if insomnia lasts for longer than 3 or 4 weeks, see your doctor to determine the cause and get treatment. Making changes to your sleep habits, such as keeping a regular sleep schedule and avoiding caffeine and alcohol at night, can help both short-term and chronic insomnia. If needed, taking sleeping pills can also help improve sleep, but medications should not be used as the only therapy."

Instead, they may have vague flu-like symptoms and fatigue. As long as a person starts treatment quickly, antibiotics usually cure the infection without other problems. Without treatment, Lyme disease can progress and cause painful joints. Rarely, it can cause memory problems, mood changes, heart rhythm problems, and other issues.     {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Nicotine is an addictive substance in tobacco that affects a person's mood. People who smoke, chew, or inhale tobacco regularly develop a dependence on nicotine. Trying to cut down or quit will trigger nicotine withdrawal. The body reacts when the supply of nicotine is cut off. It causes an intense desire for nicotine, irritable mood, headaches, and anxiety. Nicotine withdrawal can be difficult and unpleasant, but it is not harmful. It often subsides within a few weeks as the body adjusts. Treatment can help people resist the desire to use tobacco again during nicotine withdrawal. The effects of nicotine withdrawal are temporary. The benefits of quitting tobacco use will last the rest of a person's life."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Just a few hours after a person last used tobacco, nicotine withdrawal symptoms may start. They tend to get worse over the following hours and peak after two or three days. Symptoms tend to be most severe in people who were heavy users or used tobacco for many years. Nicotine withdrawal can trigger symptoms in people who have previously had depression or other psychological conditions. Nicotine withdrawal symptoms should go away within a few weeks. Taking nicotine supplements -- such as skin patches, gum, inhalers, and nasal sprays -- will reduce withdrawal symptoms and may make it easier to quit. People who quit may still have intense desires for tobacco weeks, months, or years later, especially during situations in which they used to smoke. This is not a sign of nicotine withdrawal but a psychological craving."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "80% to 90% of people who smoke are addicted to nicotine. They will have nicotine withdrawal if they try to quit."

One of the symptoms is often described as \"the worst headache of your life.\" A ruptured brain aneurysm can lead to stroke or death and is a medical emergency. Unless it ruptures, however, a brain aneurysm may cause no symptoms. If you smoke or have high blood pressure, atherosclerosis, or other risk factors, you should get screened. Detecting a brain aneurysm early makes it much easier to treat. Monitoring and surgery are the usual treatments."
    },
    {
      "heading": "EMERGENCY",
      "content": "If you have symptoms of a ruptured brain aneurysm, such as a sudden and extremely severe headache, stiff neck, nausea and vomiting, blurred or double vision, trouble walking or dizziness, loss of consciousness, sensitivity to light, and seizures, call 911."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Many aneurysms remain small and don't produce any symptoms. One that is growing may cause pain around the eye, vision problems, and partial facial paralysis as it gets larger. If it hemorrhages, you may have a sudden, severe headache, double vision, and vomiting or faint. If the brain aneurysm is small, your doctor may watch it over time to see if it grows or starts causing symptoms. If it does, you may need either surgery to cut off blood flow to the aneurysm or a non-surgical procedure that \"packs\" the aneurysm with small coils or a balloon. You'll be in the hospital for one to three days and recovery may take up to six weeks. If the aneurysm has ruptured, you'll receive emergency surgery and may be hospitalized for three or four weeks to be monitored or treated for complications."

    },

    },

    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Asthma is a lung condition that causes airways to swell and become inflamed. Asthma makes it hard to breathe. Some people may have minor symptoms, but others may have severe symptoms that interfere with daily life. Having severe asthma may put you at risk for a life-threatening asthma attack. Symptoms of an asthma attack include wheezing, chest tightness, coughing, and shortness of breath. Doctors don't know exactly what causes asthma, but genetic and environmental factors may play a role. Triggers such as smoking, air pollution, allergies, exercise, stress, GERD, and colds can bring on asthma attacks. Asthma is a lifelong condition that can be managed but not cured. Avoiding asthma triggers and taking medication help most people avoid attacks and keep symptoms under control."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Being exposed to pet dander, pollen, air pollution, or smoke can trigger an asthma attack. Having a cold or flu can also trigger attacks. Asthma attacks may come on suddenly or build slowly over several hours or days. Common symptoms include wheezing, shortness of breath, chest tightness, and coughing. Asthma symptoms can vary, and not everyone has the same symptoms all the time. Symptoms may be mild and barely noticeable, or they may interfere with daily life. Severe asthma attacks are a medical emergency. Taking daily medications, called controller medications, can help prevent asthma attacks. Once an attack has started, rescue medications help relax and open airways. Most people need both types of medication to control asthma."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "24 million people in the U.S. have asthma."

Pinworms also can enter the vagina, where they cause itching. Pinworm infection occurs most often in children between ages 5 and 10. They are easy to treat with medications, and usually are not dangerous. But often, the whole family needs to be treated to avoid the spread of pinworms."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Pinworm infections are treated easily with a single dose of one of two commonly used antiparasitic drugs. Because the eggs can survive for up to two weeks, a follow-up dose is given usually two weeks later. Pinworms are highly contagious, so members of the same household should be treated, as well. Recurrences are common. They can be prevented with careful hygiene."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Risk Factors",
      "content": "Although pinworm infections can occur at any age, they are most common in school-aged children."
    },

      "content": "More than 40 million cases of pinworm infection occur in the U.S. each year."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Prevention",
      "content": "Good hygiene can help prevent the spread of pinworms, although once a family member is infected it's difficult to contain pinworms. Teach everyone in the family to wash hands before eating or preparing meals. Keep children's fingernails short and clean, and avoid biting fingernails. Don't scratch the anal area. Wash all bedding and pajamas regularly and change underwear every day. Vacuum the children's play area often."
    },

    },

    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Heartburn happens when stomach acid backs up into your esophagus, irritating the lining of the esophagus. Usually when you swallow, a muscle around the bottom of the esophagus opens to let food go through and then closes. When it gets weak or doesn't close right, it lets stomach acid flow back into your esophagus. Heartburn causes a burning sensation in your mouth or chest or a sour taste in your mouth. Mild, occasional heartburn usually isn't a problem. Heartburn that happens frequently is called gastroesophageal reflux disorder, or GERD.  It is also possible to have GERD without symptoms of discomfort in your chest.  GERD without symptoms has been linked to causing ear infections, sinus infection, excessive mucus in the throat. Over time, GERD can wear away at your esophagus and increase your risk for esophageal cancer and other problems."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Most people have heartburn at one time or another. Mild heartburn usually gets better in a few hours. Many people can treat heartburn on their own with over-the-counter antacids and other medications. Avoiding foods that make your heartburn worse can also help. If you have heartburn more than twice a week or symptoms really bother you, you may have GERD. You may need prescription medications or surgery to treat GERD."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "About 1 in 20 people in the U.S. have chronic acid reflux, or GERD."

    {

      "content": "Treatment for heartburn and GERD usually begins with over-the-counter antacids and medications to help reduce stomach acid. You also may want to avoid the foods and drinks that make your symptoms worse. If these treatments don't relieve symptoms, you may need prescription medications, surgery, or other procedures."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "You may be able to ease the symptoms of heartburn or GERD by avoiding the foods and drinks that make your symptoms worse. You also can eat smaller meals, lose weight, quit smoking, reduce stress, and avoid lying down for two to three hours after a meal."

  "Peripheral_neuropathy": [
    {
      "heading": "Peripheral Neuropathy",
      "content": "Peripheral neuritis, neuropathy, nerve damage, nerve pain"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "n\t\t•\tDifficulty staying asleep \n\t\t•\tWeakness \n\t\t•\tPain or discomfort \n\t\t•\tNumbness or tingling"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Peripheral neuropathy is a long-term condition in which the nerves in the arms, legs, hands, and feet are damaged and don't work properly. Diabetes is the most common cause, but other conditions, injuries, infections, and medications also can cause peripheral neuropathy. Peripheral neuropathy may not have a cause. People with peripheral neuropathy often have numbness and tingling in the hands and feet, nerve pain, and lack of coordination. It also can cause difficulty moving and decreased sensation. Peripheral neuropathy can cause diarrhea, dizziness, blurred vision, impotence, and weight loss."
    },

      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Night eating syndrome (NES) is compulsive eating during the night. People with it may eat half or more of their daily food after dinner. Many people develop it around age 30, most often after a stressful event, such as a job loss, difficult pregnancy, or illness in the family. It is more common in people who are obese, have other eating disorders, or are substance abusers. Unlike eating disorders such as anorexia and bulimia, it affects women and men almost equally. It also tends to run in families. People with NES are fully conscious when they eat, unlike people with sleep-related eating disorder, who may eat while sleepwalking. Medication and counseling can help deal with it."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Night eating syndrome affects your eating, sleep, and mood. If you have it, you may feel that you need to eat after dinner until bedtime or you won't fall asleep. About two hours after you fall asleep, you may wake up feeling that you need to eat more to go back to sleep. If you don't eat, you may feel restless and agitated. You may end up eating a few small meals during the night, but no breakfast. You may delay your first meal of the day for several hours. Some people with NES diet during the day. Many people with NES are ashamed of their eating disorder or depressed, and depression in people with NES often worsens as the day goes on. Because it is not fully understood, there is no standard treatment for NES. Your doctor may suggest medication or a program of exercise and help to establish normal daytime eating patterns.   "Narcolepsy": [
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "Difficulty staying awake during day"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Narcolepsy is a brain disorder that makes people abnormally sleepy at times during the day. They may unintentionally fall asleep for periods lasting from a few seconds to several minutes, sometimes right in the middle of an activity. Narcolepsy also disrupts night-time sleep patterns, causing poor sleep. Symptoms typically appear between ages 10 and 25. But narcolepsy can be diagnosed at almost any age. Medication can help treat excessive daytime sleepiness and promote more normal sleep at night."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "You may fall asleep unintentionally during the day, sometimes in the middle of performing a task. You may also suffer sudden, temporary loss of muscle tone. It can be as minor as a drooping eyelid or severe enough to cause physical collapse. Some people also have hallucinations and temporary paralysis while falling asleep. There is no cure for narcolepsy. But treatment with medications, naps, and good sleep habits allow most people with narcolepsy to lead normal and productive lives."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "About one in 2,000 people in the U.S. have narcolepsy."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Risk Factors",
      "content": "Family history slightly increases the risk of narcolepsy."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "There is no cure for narcolepsy. But drugs can treat the most troublesome symptoms of the disorder.     },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",

      "heading": "Overview",

For most concussions, rest is the main treatment. The brain needs time to heal. Full recovery can take days, weeks, or months. Most  concussions don't cause lasting problems but a severe head injury cold trigger swelling and/or bleeding in the brain which is life-threatening. A concussion is always serious and needs medical attention."
    },
    {
      "heading": "EMERGENCY",
      "content": "Anyone with a possible concussion should see a doctor. If a person has major concussion symptoms such as loss of consciousness, confusion, frequent vomiting, blood or fluid leaking from the ears or nose, slurred speech, difficulty walking, muscle weakness, seizures, and pupils of different sizes, go to the emergency room or call 911. Babies who are crying uncontrollably or won't eat after a possible concussion should also go to the emergency room."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Some concussion symptoms may occur right after the injury. Others may not appear until hours or days later. Watch people with a possible concussion for 24 to 48 hours. Some people find that while they're recovering from a concussion, they have mild headaches, mood changes, problems with memory, and difficulty tolerating noise. These symptoms usually go away as they heal. The doctor may suggest avoiding sports, vigorous activity, tasks that require heavy concentration, and driving until a person is completely healed."
    },

    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "Anyone with a possible concussion should see a doctor. After a concussion, your doctor may tell you to take it easy. Take acetaminophen -- not aspirin, ibuprofen, ketoprofen, or naproxen -- for pain. If people with a possible concussion go to sleep, wake them up every two to three hours to make sure they're not getting worse. Always wear appropriate safety gear when playing sports."

More serious, but less common symptoms include draining lymph nodes, enlarged spleen, eye infection, and liver, bone, joint, or lung infection. Cat scratch disease is usually mild and goes away on its own in two to four months. People with severe symptoms or a weak immune system may need antibiotics to help treat the disease."

  "Benign_paroxysmal_positional_vertigo_(BPPV)": [
    {
      "heading": "Benign Paroxysmal Positional Vertigo",
      "content": "Inner ear vertigo, bppv, labyrinthitis"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "BPPV (benign paroxysmal positional vertigo) is usually a temporary condition that affects balance and your sense of position. It's brought on by changes of head position that cause a sudden sense of spinning, known as vertigo. The dizziness comes from shifts in calcium crystals in the inner ear that normally help the brain know where you are in relation to things around you. Normally, these crystals send signals to the brain that help you balance. But with BPPV the signals are exaggerated. The result is a sensation that either you or the room is spinning. BPPV may be due to labyrinthitis, an inflammation or viral infection in parts of the inner ear. Most often, its cause is unknown. Along with the sensation of spinning, or vertigo, BPPV may cause nausea and sometimes vomiting."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "BPPV becomes more common with aging. It can come on for no apparent reason. Or it can occur after a viral infection, such as a cold or flu. When it happens, you get a sudden sense of spinning. The feeling is similar to spinning in a swivel chair or being on a ride at the amusement park. Changing your head position can bring it on or make the symptoms worse. Some people experience the feeling on waking, rolling over, or getting out of bed. During an episode, driving or operating machinery can be dangerous. Often, the BPPV improves on its own with no treatment over a period of a few weeks to a few months. Treating it with medications used for motion sickness can help relieve the symptoms. Slow rotational head movements and other exercises that you can learn from a physical therapist or other health professional can also be effective in controlling symptoms. After an initial episode, BPPV may go away, only to return months or years later."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "BPPV affects about  64 of every 100,000 people in the US. It is most common among people over age 50 and is rarely seen in people younger than 3."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "Vertigo of the inner ear often clears up without any treatment. Your doctor may prescribe medications to reduce dizziness and nausea. Your doctor may also refer you to a physical therapist or other health professional, such as an ear, nose, and throat specialist. Professionals with special training can lead you through a series of head maneuvers that can shake the calcium crystals out of the inner ear canals. These maneuvers, known as Epley maneuvers, can often effectively cure vertigo of the inner ear."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "BPPV can be treated at home by waiting it out and using motion sickness medications. Have a doctor evaluate your symptoms of vertigo, especially if it lasts more than a week or the symptoms are severe."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Smoking, movement, alcohol"
    },
    {
      "heading": "When to See Your Doctor",
      "content": "Contact your doctor if symptoms don’t improve. Also, call your doctor right away if you have vertigo and a fever, stiff neck, or sensitivity to light. Those symptoms could signal a serious viral infection such as meningitis.\n\nVertigo accompanied by problems with hearing and ringing in the ears could indicate a benign tumor in the inner ear. These symptoms should be evaluated by a doctor. Do not drive yourself to see the doctor. Vertigo makes driving risky.\n\nCall 911 or get immediate medical attention if the sensation of vertigo is accompanied by any symptoms of stroke, such as: \n\t\t•\tWeakness or numbness on one side of the body \n\t\t•\tLoss of vision \n\t\t•\tDifficulty speaking"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Questions to Ask Your Doctor",

      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Type 2 diabetes occurs when the body does not make enough insulin or does not use it as it should. If there is not enough insulin, or if cells do not respond to the insulin, glucose can build up in the blood, which can cause complications, organ damage, and coma.\n\nBeing overweight increases your risk for type 2 diabetes, but not everyone who is overweight will develop the disease. Type 2 diabetes also affects people who are not overweight. Eating too much sugar won't cause diabetes, although a diet high in fat and calories will lead to weight gain."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "At first, type 2 diabetes often has no symptoms, so you can have it and not know it. The condition starts as prediabetes. This means that your glucose levels are higher than normal, with fasting blood sugars between 100 and 125. Once your fasting blood sugars are over 126, you have type 2 diabetes. You can help prevent or delay getting diabetes by losing weight, eating healthy food, and exercising more. Once you have type 2 diabetes, you need to manage it with those same lifestyle changes and medication. By keeping your glucose at a healthy level, you can lower your risk for serious health problems, including eye disease, kidney damage, nerve damage, heart disease, and stroke."

  "Heart_rhythm_disorder": [
    {
      "heading": "Heart Rhythm Disorder",
      "content": "Heart arrhythmia, cardiac ectopy, dysrrhythmia"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "n\t\t•\tDizziness \n\t\t•\tFatigue \n\t\t•\tIrregular heartbeat \n\t\t•\tNervousness \n\t\t•\tPressure or heaviness \n\t\t•\tShortness of breath \n\t\t•\tFainting \n\t\t•\tPain or discomfort \n\t\t•\tFeeling faint \n\t\t•\tLightheadedness \n\t\t•\tPalpitations (fluttering in chest) \n\t\t•\tRapid heart rate (pulse) \n\t\t•\tSlow heart rate (pulse) \n\t\t•\tWeakness (generalized)"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "Heart rhythm disorder – heart arrhythmia -- happens when the electrical impulses that control your heartbeat don't work correctly. Your heart may beat too slowly, too quickly, or have an irregular beat. Many heart rhythm problems are harmless. But some long-lasting heart rhythm disorders can cause a fluttering in the chest, shortness of breath, chest pain, or dizziness. Atrial fibrillation, when the upper chambers of the heart beat fast and out of rhythm, is a common heart rhythm disorder. It can be dangerous over time. Another type of problem, ventricular fibrillation, can cause death within minutes. It causes the lower chambers of the heart to quiver, so the heart can't pump any blood. Ventricular fibrillation is an emergency and needs to be treated with an electrical shock (defibrillator) to keep the heart working."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "You may not know you have a heart rhythm disorder unless your doctor finds a problem during an exam. And not all heart arrhythmias need treatment. It depends on the type of symptoms you have, how often they happen, and what type of heart rhythm problem you have. Most can be treated with medication, pacemakers, surgery, or other procedures."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "About 2.2 million Americans have atrial fibrillation, including 3% to 5% of people over 65."
    },
    {

    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "The aortic valve normally opens and closes as the heart beats, letting blood flow from the heart into the body's main artery. With aortic regurgitation, the valve doesn't close completely, and some blood leaks back into the heart. This makes the heart work harder to get blood to all parts of the body. Eventually, this can lead to heart failure. Leaky valves develop with age. Most people over 80 have some degree of aortic regurgitation. A leaky aortic valve can also be caused by infection, calcium deposits on the valve, or a bicuspid valve. Symptoms of aortic regurgitation typically develop over time. In rare cases, they can come on suddenly and without warning. This could be the result of an infection, dissection of the thoracic aorta, or a trauma to the valve or artery. When it happens, it can be life-threatening."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Aortic regurgitation can be present for years with little or no symptoms other than a heart murmur that can be heard with a stethoscope. At a certain point, the heart muscle cannot keep up and symptoms of heart failure develop. These include shortness of breath, a headache from high blood pressure, a whooshing sound in the chest, swelling in the feet, and an increased risk for fainting. With mild regurgitation, you'll need to see your doctor regularly so the condition can be monitored. Eventually, you may take medications to control the symptoms. At some point as the condition worsens, you may need surgery to repair or replace the valve. For many people, symptoms dramatically improve once the valve is replaced."
    },

      "content": "Millions of people in the U.S. have some degree of aortic regurgitation."
    },

You will probably need open heart surgery to replace the valve if the condition becomes severe."

  "High_blood_pressure_(hypertension)": [
    {
      "heading": "Symptoms",
      "content": "n\t\t•\tDizziness \n\t\t•\tLightheadedness \n\t\t•\tFlushed skin \n\t\t•\tHeadache \n\t\t•\tChange in vision \n\t\t•\tHigh blood pressure"
    },
    {
      "heading": "Overview",
      "content": "High blood pressure is a condition in which blood pressure stays above a healthy range and puts too much force against artery walls. Blood pressure is measured by two numbers, for example, 140/90. The top number is called systolic pressure and measures the pressure when the heart beats. The bottom number is called diastolic pressure and measures the pressure as the heart rests between beats. A person has high blood pressure if the top number is 140 or higher or if the bottom number is 90 or higher. In most cases, there's no one cause of primary high blood pressure. High blood pressure often happens with age. Other risk factors are being overweight, having a family history, heavy drinking, lack of exercise, poor diet, and other factors. Primary hypertension often develops over years. Secondary hypertension is caused by an underlying condition such as kidney problems, some medications, and tumors on the adrenal glands. Left untreated, high blood pressure can damage the heart, arteries, and kidneys and greatly increase the risk for stroke and heart attack. Eating a healthy, low-salt diet, getting regular exercise, quitting smoking, staying at a healthy weight, and taking medication can lower blood pressure to a healthy range."
    },
    {
      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "Because high blood pressure has no symptoms, it's important to have your blood pressure checked regularly, especially as you get older. If you are diagnosed with high blood pressure, you can keep it under control and lower your risk for serious health problems by making healthy lifestyle changes and taking blood pressure medications as directed."
    },
    {
      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "A third of adults in the U.S. have high blood pressure."
    },
    {
      "heading": "Treatment",
      "content": "You may be able to lower blood pressure by making lifestyle changes. If those aren't enough, your doctor will prescribe medication.     },

      "heading": "What to Expect",
      "content": "A Meniere's attack may start with a roaring or ringing sound called tinnitus and a feeling of fullness in the ear. Vertigo comes on suddenly, making it hard to walk or stand upright. Nausea and vomiting may occur with vertigo. Attacks can last from a few minutes to a few hours. Once vertigo has passed, most people feel fatigued and need to sleep. Most people have no symptoms between attacks, although some have lingering dizziness. Some people go long periods between attacks and others have several over a few days. A Meniere's attack can come on so suddenly that the person falls to the ground. These are called drop attacks. Over time, tinnitus occurs constantly and hearing loss becomes permanent. A low-sodium diet and diuretics can help prevent attacks."
    },

      "heading": "How Common",
      "content": "About 615,000 people in the U.S. have Meniere's disease."
    },

      "content": "Treatment for Meniere's disease includes: \n\t\t•\tA low-sodium diet \n\t\t•\tDiuretics (water pills) \n\t\t•\tMedications to lessen dizziness, such as anti-motion drugs \n\t\t•\tMedication to help control nausea \n\t\t•\tSurgery if medications don't work and attacks are disabling"
    },

      "heading": "Self Care",
      "content": "Self-care for a Meniere's attack involves waiting it out at home and taking anti-motion drugs. Sitting still and looking at one place can help control vertigo."
    },

      "heading": "Made Worse By",
      "content": "Smoking, movement, alcohol, caffeine, chocolate"
    },

      "content": "If symptoms last longer than a day or the symptoms are severe or you can't keep fluids down because of nausea and vomiting, you should see your doctor."

    },

      "heading": "Diagnosed By",
      "content": "Your doctor will take your medical history and perform a physical exam, along with an ear exam, hearing tests, and motion tests. Your doctor may also do a CT scan or MRI to rule out other causes."

      "heading": "Risk Factors",
